However, we did not understand the skillful means of adaptive teaching according to circumstances. Upon first hearing the Buddha's teaching, we encountered it and immediately generated faith, contemplated it, and claimed realization.

"World-Honored One, since ancient times I have continuously and throughout day and night reproached myself. Yet now, having heard from the Buddha teachings never before heard, unprecedented dharmas, I have severed all doubt and regret. My body and mind are at peace, my heart is utterly tranquil. Today I truly understand that I am a Buddha-child, born from the Buddha's mouth, born from the dharma, and possessed of the Buddha-dharma inheritance."[^2]

---

## Śāriputra's Verse of Joy

Then Śāriputra, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse:

*Having heard this dharma-sound,*  
*I have obtained what was never before experienced,*  
*My heart overflows with great joy,*  
*And all my nets of doubt have been severed.*  

*In the past, receiving the Buddha's teaching,*  
*I did not lose the great vehicle,*  
*The Buddha's sound is supremely rare,*  
*Able to remove all sentient beings' afflictions.*  

*I have already exhausted all outflows,*  
*And this hearing has removed my distress.*  
*I dwelt in valleys*  
*Or beneath forest trees,*  

*Whether sitting or walking in meditation,*  
*I constantly contemplated this matter,*  
*Grieving deeply and reproaching myself:*  
*How could I deceive myself?*  

*We too are Buddha-children,*  
*Having entered into the unconditioned dharma together,*  
*Yet I have been unable in the future,*  
*To expound the supreme path.*  

*The thirty-two marks of gold,*  
*The ten powers and all liberations,*  
*Shared within one dharma,*  
*Yet I did not obtain this.*  

*The eighty wonderful characteristics,*  
*The eighteen dharmas unique to Buddhas,*  
*And such meritorious qualities as these,*  
*I have altogether lost.*  

*During my solitary practice, walking,*  
*I saw the Buddha in the great assembly,*  
*His renown filling the ten directions,*  
*Bringing immeasurable benefit to all beings.*  

*I contemplated how I had lost this benefit,*  
*And knew that I had deceived myself.*  
*Constantly throughout day and night,*  
*I contemplated this matter,*  

*Wishing to question the World-Honored One,*  
*Whether I had lost or not lost this,*  
*I constantly saw the World-Honored One,*  
*Praising the various Bodhisattvas.*  

*Because of this, throughout day and night,*  
*I deliberated upon this matter.*  
*Now, hearing the Buddha's voice,*  
*Teaching the dharma according to circumstances,*  

*The unconditioned, inconceivable dharma,*  
*That brings all beings to enlightenment.*  
*I was originally attached to perverted views,*  
*A teacher to all the Brahmin ascetics.*  

*The World-Honored One knew my mind,*  
*And uprooted my perverted views by teaching nirvāṇa.*  
*I have completely eliminated perverted views,*  
*And realized the dharma of emptiness.*  

*At that time, my mind believed,*  
*That I had attained extinction.*  
*Yet now I have suddenly awakened,*  
*That this is not true extinction.*  

*When I obtain Buddhahood,*  
*Possessing the thirty-two marks,*  
*The devas, humans, yakshas,*  
*And dragon-beings showing reverence,*  

*Only then can it be truly said,*  
*That extinction has been completed without remainder.*  
*The Buddha, in the great assembly,*  
*Has taught that I shall become a Buddha.*  

*Having heard such dharma-sound,*  
*All my doubt and regret have been severed.*  
*When I first heard what the Buddha taught,*  
*My heart harbored great astonishment and doubt,*  

*"Could this be the work of Māra,*  
*Creating a false Buddha, disturbing my mind?"*  
*The Buddha, through various causes and conditions,*  
*Parables and skillfully chosen words, spoke,*  

*And my heart became calm as the ocean,*  
*My nets of doubt were severed upon hearing.*  
*The Buddha spoke of past world-ages,*  
*Countless Buddhas who had passed away,*  

*Who dwell within skillful means,*  
*And all taught this very dharma.*  
*The Buddhas of the present and future,*  
*Are boundless in number,*  

*And also through skillful means,*  
*Expound this dharma likewise.*  
*Just as the World-Honored One of today,*  
*From birth and renunciation of family life,*  

*Attained the path and turned the dharma-wheel,*  
*Also teaching through skillful means.*  
*The World-Honored One speaks the true path—*  
*Māra has no such accomplishment.*  

*Therefore, I know with certainty,*  
*That this is not the work of Māra.*  
*I had fallen into nets of doubt,*  
*Thinking it was Māra's doing.*  

*Upon hearing the Buddha's gentle and soft voice,*  
*Profoundly mysterious and supremely subtle,*  
*Expounding the pure dharma,*  
*My heart was filled with great joy.*  

*Doubt and regret have forever been severed,*  
*And I dwell firmly in true wisdom.*  
*I shall certainly become a Buddha,*  
*Honored by devas and humans,*  

*Turn the supreme dharma-wheel,*  
*And teach the various Bodhisattvas.*  

---

## The Buddha's Prophecy for Śāriputra

Then the Buddha addressed Śāriputra: "Now I speak before the assembly of devas, humans, monks, and Brahmins: I previously, for immeasurable billions of eons, in the presence of twenty billion Buddhas, for the sake of achieving the supreme path, continually taught and transformed you. You also, for countless nights, followed me and received my teaching.

"Because I guided you through skillful means, you were born within my teaching. Śāriputra, in the past I taught you to aspire to the Buddha path. You have now forgotten this, and thus you presume you have already attained extinction. Now I wish to cause you to recollect your original vow and the path you cultivated. For this reason, for all the Śrāvakas I teach this great-vehicle sutra, called the *Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma Lotus Flower*, the teaching for Bodhisattvas, protected and remembered by all the Buddhas."[^3]

---

## Śāriputra's Future Buddhahood

"Śāriputra, in the future world, after passing through immeasurable, boundless, inconceivable eons, having made offerings to countless billions of Buddhas, upheld the correct dharma, perfected the bodhisattva path, you shall become a Buddha with the title Tathāgata of Radiant Flower, Worthy of Offerings, Possessor of Perfect Knowledge, Possessor of Perfect Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed Guide, Trainer of Beings, Teacher of Devas and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.

"The land will be named Pure Radiance, its earth level and even, supremely pure and beautifully adorned, secure and prosperous, with abundance of devas and humans. Its ground shall be made of lapis lazuli, with eight crossways adorned with golden boundaries. Throughout the realm shall grow seven-jeweled trees, eternally bearing flowers and fruit. The Tathāgata of Radiant Flower shall also teach the three vehicles to sentient beings."[^4]

---

## The Golden Age of Radiant Flower Buddha

"Śāriputra, at the time of this Buddha's emergence, although it will not be an evil age, because of his original vow, he will teach the three-vehicle dharma. The eon shall be named 'Great Jeweled Adornment.' Why is it called 'Great Jeweled Adornment'? Because the beings considered the great jewels of that realm are the Bodhisattvas themselves.[^5]

"Those Bodhisattvas shall be boundless, immeasurable, and inconceivable—beyond the capacity of calculation, parable, or comparison. Only the Buddha's wisdom has the capacity to know them. When they desire to go about, precious flowers manifest beneath their feet.

"These Bodhisattvas are not newly-aspiring ones but have long planted roots of virtue and cultivated the Brahmin conduct purely in the presence of countless billions of Buddhas. They are continuously praised by all Buddhas, constantly cultivate Buddha-wisdom, possess great supernatural powers, skillfully understand the gateways to all dharmas, are direct and without deception, and have fixed, unwavering intentions. Such Bodhisattvas fill that Buddha-realm entirely."[^6]

---

## THE PARABLE OF THE BURNING HOUSE

## The Setting and the Fire

"Śāriputra, if in a country, city, or village there is a great householder—elderly, with wealth without measure, possessing much land, houses, servants, and retainers. His house is vast with only a single gate. Many people—one hundred, two hundred, up to five hundred—dwell within it. The buildings are decayed and deteriorated, the walls cracked and crumbling, the pillars at their foundations rotting and corrupting, the roof beams tilting dangerously. All around simultaneously, fire suddenly erupts, burning the entire dwelling.[^7]

"The householder's children—ten, twenty, or as many as thirty—are within this house. The householder sees the great fire rising from all four sides, is immediately seized with tremendous alarm, and thinks within himself:

"'Although I am able to escape safely from this burning gate, my children, being within the burning house, are absorbed in play and amusement, unaware and unknowing, neither startled nor fearful. The fire approaches their bodies, the pain is acute, yet their minds harbor no loathing, nor do they harbor any thought of escaping.'"[^8]

---

## The Father's Dilemma and First Attempt

"Śāriputra, the householder then thinks: 'My body possesses strength and skill. I should use my garments or a cloth to pull the children out of the house.' Then again he thinks: 'This house has only one gate, and it is narrow and small. The children are very young, without understanding or awareness, clinging to their playing places. When they come out, they might fall down or be burned by the fire. I must describe to them the frightening nature of this situation—this house is already burning, and it is fitting that you quickly exit, so that the fire does not burn and harm you.'

"Having this thought, he carefully tells all his children: 'You must quickly exit!' Though the father is compassionate and entices them with gentle words, the children, absorbed in play, do not believe him, are neither startled nor afraid, and have no intention of exiting. They do not know what fire is, what the house is, what being lost means, but only run about in play to the east and west, glancing at their father."[^9]

---

## The Father's Skilful Means

"At that time the householder immediately thinks: 'This house is greatly burning in flames. If I and my children do not exit in time, we shall all be burned. Now I must establish skillful means to enable my children to escape this danger.'

"The father knows that each of his children previously harbored various preferences for rare and wonderful playthings and objects, which their feelings naturally delighted in. He tells them:

"'The playthings and precious objects that delight you are rare and difficult to obtain. If you do not take them, you will surely regret it later. There are here various kinds of sheep-carts, deer-carts, and ox-carts, now outside the gate, which you can use for play. Come quickly out of this burning house, and according to your wishes, I shall give you each one of these carts.'

"At that moment, the children, hearing their father speak of these rare playthings suited to their desires, each became eager and sharp in mind, competing with one another and pushing forward in rivalry, hastening to run out of the burning house.[^10]

"At this time the householder, seeing his children safely escape, all seated in the open space at the four crossroads, unobstructed and secure, his heart at peace and overflowing with joy, thinks to himself:

"Then his children each address their father: 'Father, the playthings and precious objects you previously promised us—the sheep-carts, deer-carts, and ox-carts—we wish you to give them to us now.'"[^11]

---

## The Gift of the Great Carriage

"Śāriputra, at that time the householder bestows upon each child one great carriage. That carriage is tall and spacious, adorned and decorated with multitudinous precious materials, surrounded by railings, with bells suspended from all four sides. From its top hangs a jeweled canopy, also decorated and adorned with rare and various treasures. Jeweled ropes interweave, and precious flower-garlands hang down. Soft fabrics are thickly spread within, and cushioned with crimson pillows are arranged for reclining.

"It is drawn by white oxen, with lustrous and pure skin, beautiful in form, possessing great strength and power, steady and even in gait, swift as wind. Many servants accompany and protect it. Why is this?[^fn_white_ox_cart]

[^fn_white_ox_cart]: **The Great White Ox Cart (大白牛車):** A critical distinction must be made between the "Ox Cart" (*niu che*) promised earlier (symbolizing the Bodhisattva vehicle among the Three Vehicles) and this "Great White Ox Cart" (*da bai niu che*) actually given. The "Great White Ox Cart" represents the *Ekayāna* (One Buddha Vehicle) which transcends and encompasses all three provisional vehicles. Kumārajīva's translation explicitly adds "Great White" to distinguish the ultimate reality from the provisional promise.

"This great householder possesses wealth without limit, and all his various treasuries are completely full and overflowing. Yet he thinks:

"'My wealth is without end—it is not fitting that I give my children inferior small carts. All these youths are my sons, and I love them without partiality. I possess such seven-jeweled great carts without measure, and it is fitting that I give them with equal mind to each, without favoritism. Why is this? Even if I were to distribute these possessions throughout an entire realm, there would still be no exhaustion—how much more so for my sons?'

"At this time each of the children, riding the great carriage, experienced what they had never before experienced, and what they had not originally expected."[^13]

---

## The Buddha's Application

"Śāriputra, what do you think of this matter? Is the householder's giving of these precious great carts to the children deceptive in any way?"[^14]

Śāriputra replied: "No, World-Honored One. The householder merely caused his children to escape the fire-danger and preserve their lives—this is not deceptive. Why? Because preserving their bodies and lives, they have already obtained the playthings they desired, much more so through skillful means to rescue them from the burning house. World-Honored One, even if the householder had not given them even the smallest single cart, it would still not be deceptive. Why? Because the householder previously formed this intention: 'I shall employ skillful means to enable my children to escape.' By this cause and condition, there is no deception. How much more so when the householder, knowing his wealth is immeasurable, wishes to benefit his children and gives them equally great carts."[^15]

---

## The Central Teaching: The Burning House and the Three Worlds

Then the Buddha said to Śāriputra: "Excellent! Excellent! As you have spoken. Śāriputra, the Tathāgata is likewise the father of all the world.[^16]

"The Tathāgata has completely exhausted all fears, afflictions, distress, sorrow, and ignorance and darkness—having realized infinite wisdom and knowledge, fearlessness, great supernatural power, and wisdom-power. The Tathāgata has perfected the perfection of skillful means and wisdom, great compassion and great loving-kindness, never ceasing in endeavor, constantly seeking to benefit all beings.[^17]

"The Tathāgata, being born within the three-world decaying house, in order to liberate sentient beings from birth, old age, sickness, death, sorrow, affliction, pain, ignorance, and darkness—from the fire of the three poisons (greed, hatred, delusion)—teaches the dharma, enabling them to attain anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi.[^18]

"The Tathāgata sees sentient beings burned by birth, old age, sickness, death, sorrow, affliction, and pain. Because of the five desires and material wealth, they undergo various sufferings. Because of their greedy attachment and pursuit, they experience present suffering, and in the future will experience the suffering of hell-beings, animals, and hungry ghosts. If born among devas and humans, they experience poverty, suffering, separation from loved ones, meeting with those they hate—all manner of such various sufferings.

"Sentient beings are submerged within these sufferings, yet take joy in their entertainments and games, unaware and unknowing, neither startled nor afraid, generating no loathing and seeking no liberation. In this three-world burning house, they run about to east and west. Though they encounter great suffering, they do not regard it as a calamity.

"Śāriputra, the Buddha, having seen this, forms this thought: 'I am the father of sentient beings—I should free them from their suffering and hardship, and grant them the joy of infinite, boundless Buddha-wisdom, enabling them to engage in play.'[^19]"

---

## The Buddha's Internal Deliberation

"Śāriputra, the Tathāgata again forms this thought: 'If I merely employ supernatural power and wisdom-power, abandoning skillful means, and for sentient beings praise the Tathāgata's wisdom, fearlessness, and powers, sentient beings would not thereby be liberated. Why? These sentient beings have not yet escaped birth, old age, sickness, death, and sorrow—they are burned by the three-world burning house. How could they comprehend Buddha-wisdom?'[^20]"

---

## The Buddha's Skilful Teaching of the Three Vehicles

"Śāriputra, as the householder, though possessed of physical strength and skill, did not employ it, but only through persistent skillful means labored to rescue his children from the burning house, and afterward gave them precious great carts—the Tathāgata is likewise:

"Although possessing power and fearlessness, the Tathāgata does not employ it. Rather, through wisdom and skillful means, within the three-world burning house, the Tathāgata liberates sentient beings, teaching the three vehicles—the Śrāvaka Vehicle, the Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, and the Buddha Vehicle—and speaks as follows:[^21]

"'You must not take pleasure in dwelling within the three-world burning house. Do not covet the coarse and inferior—forms, sounds, smells, tastes, and tactile sensations. If you grasp at them with craving and attachment, you will be burned. You must quickly emerge from the three worlds and attain the three vehicles—the Śrāvaka Vehicle, the Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, and the Buddha Vehicle. I give you my assurance of this matter; it shall never be false. You need only diligently cultivate mindfulness.'

"The Tathāgata, through these skillful means, encourages sentient beings forward, and again speaks: 'You should know that these three-vehicle dharmas are all praised by the sages, are free and unbound, with nothing to depend upon or seek. By means of these three vehicles, employing the unconditioned roots, powers, awakening-factors, path-factors, meditation, liberation, samadhi, and such, you shall entertain yourselves and obtain countless security, ease, and happiness.'"[^22]

---

## The Three Vehicles Explained

"Śāriputra, if there are sentient beings who possess inner wisdom-nature, who hear the dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, generate faith and receive it, diligently cultivate, and speedily wish to escape the three worlds, seeking nirvāṇa for themselves—this is called the Śrāvaka Vehicle, like those children who sought the sheep-carts and exited the burning house.[^23]

"If there are sentient beings who hear the dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, generate faith and receive it, diligently cultivate, seek the wisdom arising of itself, delight in solitary peace, and deeply understand the causes and conditions of all dharmas—this is called the Pratyekabuddha Vehicle, like those children who sought the deer-carts and exited the burning house.[^24]

"If there are sentient beings who hear the dharma from the Buddha, the World-Honored One, generate faith and receive it, diligently cultivate, seek the wisdom-knowing-all-aspects, Buddha-wisdom, wisdom-arising-of-itself, wisdom-without-teacher, the Tathāgata's wisdom and vision, the Buddha's power and fearlessness, who have compassion for and give ease to countless sentient beings, who benefit devas and humans, and liberate all beings—this is called the Great Vehicle. Bodhisattvas seek this vehicle, and therefore are called Mahāsattvas (Great Beings), like those children who sought the ox-carts and exited the burning house."[^25]

---

## The Ultimate Gift: The One Vehicle

"Śāriputra, as the householder, having seen his children safely escape from the burning house and arrive at the fearless place, knowing his wealth is without measure, grants to each child these great vehicles as equal gifts—the Tathāgata is likewise:

"Being the father of all sentient beings, when the Tathāgata sees countless billions of sentient beings, having escaped the suffering and fear of the three-world dangerous path through the Buddha-teaching gate, having attained the joy of nirvāṇa, the Tathāgata at that time forms this thought:

"'I possess infinite, boundless wisdom, fearlessness, and all the Buddha-dharma treasuries. All these sentient beings are my children. I shall equally grant them the great vehicle, and not allow any being to attain liberation alone.'

"All sentient beings are liberated by means of the Tathāgata's nirvāṇa. All these sentient beings who have escaped the three worlds are provided with the treasuries for entertainment of all the Buddhas—meditative absorptions, liberations, and such—all of which are of one characteristic, one type, praised by the sages, and capable of producing the purest, most supreme joy."[^26]

---

## The Resolution: No Deception in Skilful Means

"Śāriputra, as the householder initially employed three carts to entice his children, and subsequently gave them only the great carriage, with precious treasures and adornment, the most secure and supreme—yet that householder harbored no deception.

"The Tathāgata is likewise—there is no deception. The Tathāgata initially teaches the three vehicles to guide and transform sentient beings, and subsequently liberates all through the great vehicle alone. Why is this? Because the Tathāgata possesses infinite wisdom, fearlessness, and all dharma-treasuries, and is able to grant the great-vehicle dharma to all sentient beings—only they do not all have the capacity to receive it.[^27]

"Śāriputra, by means of this cause and condition, you should understand: The Buddhas, through the power of skillful means, within the One Buddha Vehicle, make distinctions and teach the three vehicles."[^28]

---

## The Complete Vision of the Buddha's Teaching

Then the Buddha, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse elaborating the entire parable with vivid detail and concluding:

*The World-Honored One is like this—*  
*Honored among all sages,*  
*Father of all sentient beings.*  
*All are my children,*  

*Deeply clinging to worldly pleasures,*  
*Lacking wisdom.*  
*The three worlds are without peace,*  
*Like a burning house,*  

*Full of all manner of suffering,*  
*Extremely fearful.*  
*Eternally possessing birth, old age, sickness, death, and sorrow—*  
*Such fires continuously blaze.*  

*The Tathāgata has already escaped the three-world burning house,*  
*Dwelling in tranquility, in peaceful solitude.*  
*Now this three worlds is wholly mine,*  
*And all its sentient beings are my children.*  

*Yet this realm contains many sufferings,*  
*Only I alone am able to rescue and protect them.*  
*Though I teach continually, they do not believe and accept.*  
*Regarding worldly desires and attachments, they cling deeply.*  

*By means of these skillful means,*  
*I teach the three vehicles,*  
*Enabling sentient beings to understand the three-world suffering,*  
*Opening and revealing the path of transcendence.*  

*If these children's minds become determined,*  
*Perfecting the three clear-knowings and six supernatural powers,*  
*Some obtain the pratyekabuddha realization or become non-retrogressive Bodhisattvas.*  
*You, Śāriputra, I teach to sentient beings,*  

*Through this parable,*  
*The One Buddha Vehicle—*  
*If all of you can generate faith in this teaching,*  
*Then all shall certainly attain the Buddha path.*  

*This vehicle is subtle and wonderful,*  
*Pure and supreme,*  
*In all the worlds it has no superior—*  
*Praised and honored by all Buddhas and sentient beings,*  

*Worthy of reverence and worship.*  
*Possessing countless billions of supernatural powers, liberations,*  
*Concentrations, wisdom, and other Buddha-dharmas,*  
*This vehicle grants to all children,*  

*Day and night, throughout eons,*  
*Constant play and entertainment,*  
*Together with all Bodhisattvas and śrāvaka assemblies,*  
*Riding this precious vehicle,*  

*Directly reaching enlightenment.*  
*By this cause and condition,*  
*Seeking throughout the ten directions,*  
*No other vehicle exists,*  

*Except the Buddha's skillful means.*  
*Tell Śāriputra:*  
*You and all others are my children—*  
*I am your father.*  

*Throughout countless eons,*  
*You have all been burned by manifold sufferings.*  
*I have completely rescued and liberated you,*  
*Enabling you to escape the three worlds.*  

*Though I previously taught*  
*That you have attained extinction—*  
*Merely exhausting birth and death—*  
*In truth, you have not been extinguished.*  

*What now remains to be accomplished*  
*Is only Buddha-wisdom.*  
*If any Bodhisattva in this assembly,*  
*Can listen wholeheartedly to the true dharma of all Buddhas,*  

*All Buddhas, the World-Honored Ones,*  
*Though employing skillful means,*  
*Transform all sentient beings into Bodhisattvas.*  
*If there are those of limited wisdom,*  

*Deeply attached to love and desire,*  
*For such beings I teach the suffering-truth.*  
*Sentient beings' hearts delight,*  
*Obtaining what has never been obtained,*  

*The Buddha's teaching of the suffering-truth—*  
*Truly without deception.*  
*If there are sentient beings*  
*Who do not know suffering's origin,*  

*Deeply attached to suffering's cause,*  
*Unable to abandon it for even a moment—*  
*For such beings, I skillfully teach the path.*  
*The cause of all suffering is greed and desire;*  

*If greed and desire are eliminated,*  
*There is nowhere for them to depend—*  
*Destroying all suffering completely,*  
*This is named the third truth.*  

*In order to extinguish the truth of cessation,*  
*One practices the path,*  
*Severing all suffering's bonds—*  
*This is called obtaining liberation.*  

---

## WARNINGS REGARDING THE LOTUS SUTRA'S TRANSMISSION

"Śāriputra, you should not proclaim this dharma-seal of mine carelessly in places you travel. If there is one who hears, generates joy, and accepts it respectfully, understand that such a person shall attain non-retrogression. If any being generates faith and accepts this sutra-dharma, understand that such a person has already seen the Buddhas of the past, shown them respect and reverence, and also heard this dharma.

"Whoever can generate faith in what you teach shall see me, shall see you, and shall see the bhikṣu assembly and all the Bodhisattvas. This Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma is for those of profound wisdom. The shallow-minded, upon hearing it, become confused and do not understand. All the Śrāvakas and Pratyekabuddhas are unable to comprehend this sutra.

"Śāriputra, you too only enter this sutra through faith—how much more so for other Śrāvakas? Moreover, Śāriputra, you should not teach this sutra to the arrogant, the lazy, those with the view of self, nor to those common folk of shallow understanding who are deeply attached to the five desires. For those who do not believe and slander this sutra shall reap grave karmic consequences."

---

# Chapter 4: Faith And Understanding

## The Four Great Disciples' Joy and Recognition

At that time, the wise Subhūti, Mahākātyāyana, Mahākāśyapa, and Mahāmaudgalyāyana, having heard from the Buddha this unprecedented teaching, with the World-Honored One granting prophecy of anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi to Śāriputra, their hearts were moved with wonder and delight. They immediately rose from their seats, straightened their robes, uncovered their right shoulders in respectful posture, knelt upon their right knees, unified their minds by placing their palms together in reverent salutation, bowed deeply in respect, gazed upon the Buddha's countenance with undivided attention, and addressed the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, we are elders among the assembled monks, advanced in age, and we have long believed that we had already attained nirvāṇa and had no further capacity for spiritual attainment. We harbored no aspiration to advance toward anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi.

"World-Honored One, in ages past when you taught the dharma at length, we sat in the assembly, but our bodies were weary and lethargic. We contemplated only emptiness, signlessness, and non-activity. Regarding the Bodhisattva dharma—the display of supernatural powers, the purification of Buddha-lands, and the perfection of sentient beings—our hearts did not delight in these teachings. Why was this?

"The World-Honored One caused us to escape from the three worlds and attain the realization of nirvāṇa. Moreover, at this time we are advanced in age. Toward the Buddha's teaching and transformation of Bodhisattvas aspiring to anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi, we have not generated even a single thought of joy.

"Yet now, before the Buddha, we have heard the prophecy that we too shall attain anuttara-samyak-saṃbodhi as Śrāvakas (voice-hearers). Our hearts are extremely joyful—we have experienced what we had never experienced before. We did not anticipate that at this time we would suddenly hear such a rare teaching. We deeply rejoice and feel that we have obtained great benefit, acquiring precious treasures without having sought them."[^1]

---

## The Parable of the Prodigal Son: Introduction

"World-Honored One, we now wish to speak in parable to clarify this meaning:

"There was a man who, in his youth, ignorant and without understanding, abandoned his father and fled, dwelling in another land for a long time—ten, twenty, or even fifty years.[^2] In his advancing age, he became impoverished and, wandering in all directions seeking clothing and food, gradually traveled and eventually arrived near his native land.

"His father, having long sought to find his son without success, had settled in a certain city. His household was immensely wealthy, with treasures beyond measure—gold, silver, lapis lazuli, coral, amber, crystal, pearls—all his storehouses completely filled and overflowing. He had numerous servants, officials, retainers, and common people. He possessed countless elephants, horses, chariots, ox-carts, and sheep. His wealth was engaged in business, spreading throughout other lands, and there were many merchants and traders. His father's thoughts constantly turned to his lost son.

"At that time, the impoverished son, seeking employment and wandering through villages and traveling through districts, eventually arrived at the city where his father had settled. His father had long mourned his son, and he and his son had been separated for more than fifty years. Yet the father never spoke of this matter to others. He merely thought within himself, harboring regret in his heart. He contemplated: 'I am old and approaching the end of life. I possess abundant wealth and treasures—gold, silver, precious jewels—my storehouses are completely full. But I have no son to whom I can entrust these riches. One day I shall die, and my wealth shall scatter and be lost with nothing to which it can be consigned. This is why I am constantly concerned and every moment remember my son.'

"Furthermore, he formed this thought: 'If I could only obtain my son and entrust him with my wealth, I would be at ease and happiness, without further worries.'"[^3]

---

## The Encounter: The Son Becomes Afraid

"World-Honored One, at that time the impoverished son, having hired himself to various employers, eventually arrived at his father's dwelling. He stood beside the gate. From a distance, he saw his father, seated upon a magnificent throne, his feet resting upon a precious footrest. Brahmins, nobles, and merchants all paid him respectful reverence. His body was adorned with pearls and precious ornaments worth thousands of millions of coins. Officials, servants, and retainers held white fly-whisks, standing to his left and right. A jeweled canopy was suspended above him, with precious flower banners hanging down. Fragrant water had been sprinkled on the ground, and precious flowers were scattered about. Precious objects were arrayed in display, brought in and taken out for distribution. Such was the variety of his magnificent adornment, and his authority and dignity were specially exalted.

"The impoverished son, seeing his father possessed of such great power and majesty, immediately became filled with fear and regret for having come to this place. He secretly formed this thought:

"'This man may be a king or the equal of a king. He is certainly not a place where I can earn a livelihood through manual labor. It would be better for me to go to a poor village where I might have a place to exert my strength, where food and clothing can be easily obtained. If I remain here for a long time, I might be constrained and forced to labor.'

"Forming this thought, he hastily fled."[^4]

---

## The Father's Recognition and Gentle Retrieval

"At that time the wealthy householder, seated upon his throne, recognized his son immediately and his heart was filled with great joy. He formed this thought:

"'Now I have someone to whom I can entrust my wealth and treasures. I have constantly thought of this son, yet had no means to see him. Unexpectedly, he has come—this accords perfectly with my wish. Although I am advanced in age, my desires remain strong.'

"He immediately sent attendants to pursue and bring back his son. At that moment, the messenger hastily ran after the poor son to capture him. The poor son, startled and alarmed, cried out in protest:

"'I have committed no offense against you—why do you seize me?'

"The messenger held him even more tightly and forcibly dragged him back. At that time, the poor son, thinking that he had committed no transgression yet was being imprisoned and seized, believed his death was certain, and his terror increased. He became unconscious and collapsed to the ground.

"The father, observing this from a distance, instructed the messenger:

"'Release this person! Do not forcibly bring him. Sprinkle cold water on his face to revive him, and do not speak to him further.'

"Why was this? The father understood that his son, possessed of inferior spiritual capacity, was intimidated by the father's power and wealth, which the son found difficult to comprehend. Although the father clearly knew this was indeed his son, he employed skilful means, not telling other people that this was his son.

"The messenger told the son:

"'I now release you. Go wherever you wish.'

"The poor son was delighted to obtain what he had not expected. He rose from the ground and went to a poor village, seeking employment and food."[^5]

---

## The Father's Strategy: Gradual Approach

"At that time, the householder, desiring to entice and guide his son through the employment of skilful means, secretly sent two people of humble appearance, without power or prestige:

"'You may go to that place and gradually speak to the poor son, saying: "This place has work available. We shall pay you double wages. If the poor son accepts, bring him here and have him work. If he asks what kind of work it is, tell him: 'We shall hire you to clean excrement. We two shall also work with you.'"'

"The two messengers immediately sought out the poor son, and having found him, explained everything in detail. At that time, the poor son, first receiving payment, immediately began cleaning excrement with them.

"His father, observing his son, felt compassion and was amazed. On another day, peering through a window, the father saw his son—thin and gaunt, his appearance withered and shrunk, his body covered with filth and dust, unclean. Immediately the father removed his ornaments, fine clothing, and adornments, and instead put on coarse, worn, dirty garments, his body covered with dust. Holding a dung-removal implement in his right hand, with an expression showing reverence, he instructed the workers:

"'You all must work diligently without laziness!'

"By employing skilful means in this way, he was able to draw near his son. Later, he spoke to the son:

"'Listen, young man! You should continue this work here. Do not leave to seek other employment. I shall increase your wages. Whatever you need—bowls, rice, noodles, salt, vinegar, and such things—do not hesitate to ask. We have old, worn servants who can provide whatever you need. Make yourself comfortable. I am to you as a father. Do not worry further. Why is this? I am old and advanced in years, while you are young and vigorous. When you work, you show neither deception nor laziness, neither anger nor complaint. I see no such faults in you as I see in other workers. From this time forward, I shall regard you as a son.'

"At that moment, the householder gave him a new name, calling him 'child.' At that time, the poor son, although delighted by this favorable treatment, still regarded himself as a hired servant. Because of this, for twenty years, he continued to clean excrement. After this period had passed, his mind gradually grew familiar and trusting. He came and went without hindrance, yet he still remained in his former dwelling place."[^6]

---

## The Father's Deathbed Revelation

"World-Honored One, at that time the householder became ill, and knowing that death was approaching, he spoke to his son:

"'I now possess abundant gold, silver, and precious treasures, and my storehouses are completely filled. I know precisely how much is there and what should be given and taken. You should understand all of this. My heart is thus. You should comprehend this intention. Why? Now I and you are not different—you should apply your heart fully so that nothing is lost.'

"At that time the poor son, having received the instruction, took charge of all the treasures, gold, silver, precious jewels, and storehouses, yet harbored no hope of taking even a single meal's worth. Yet his dwelling place still remained his former location, and he had not yet abandoned his sense of inferiority. After another brief period, the father understood his son's mind was gradually becoming broader and more expansive, and he had achieved a noble aspiration, scorning his former inferior thoughts.

"At the time approaching the father's death, he summoned his son together with his relatives, the king, great ministers, nobles, and wealthy merchants—all having assembled—and he himself made this declaration:

"'All you gentlemen should know: This is my son, whom I fathered. In a certain city, he abandoned me and fled. For more than fifty years, impoverished and suffering hardship, he has been lost. His original name is [such and such], and my name is [such and such]. Long ago in my native city, filled with concern, I sought for him, but suddenly, unexpectedly, I encountered and found him in this place. This is truly my son, and I am truly his father. All my wealth and possessions are now his. All that I previously gave out and took in, he now knows.'

"World-Honored One, at that time the poor son, hearing these words of his father, was suddenly overwhelmed with great joy and experienced what he had never experienced before. He formed this thought:

"'I never harbored any thought of seeking or hoping for anything, yet now this treasury of precious wealth has naturally come to me.'"[^7]

---

## The Interpretation: The Buddha's Application

"World-Honored One, the wealthy householder is the Tathāgata. We all, in like manner, are Buddha-children. The Tathāgata constantly speaks of us as his children.

"World-Honored One, we have undergone the three sufferings within birth and death, experiencing various afflictions, confusion, and ignorance, delighting in and clinging to the small dharma. Today the World-Honored One has caused us to contemplate and abandon the excrement of conceptual elaboration regarding all dharmas. We diligently cultivated within this, obtaining the single day's wage of nirvāṇa, and, having obtained this, our hearts were greatly joyful. We believed this was a great attainment and said to ourselves: 'Through diligent cultivation in the Buddha's dharma, what we have obtained is abundant.'

"However, the World-Honored One previously knew that our hearts were attached to inferior desires and delighted in the small dharma. He therefore allowed us to cease, without making distinctions, that you would all share in the treasury of the Tathāgata's wisdom and vision. The World-Honored One, through the power of skilful means, explained the Buddha-wisdom, and we, from the Buddha, obtained the single day's wage of nirvāṇa, regarding it as a great attainment. Toward this great vehicle, we have harbored no aspirational seeking.

"We likewise, because of the Tathāgata's wisdom, have disclosed and explained the Bodhisattva dharma to the various Bodhisattvas, yet we ourselves have harbored no aspiration. Why? Because the Buddha knew that our hearts delight in the small dharma, through the power of skilful means and following our capacities, the Buddha taught us. Yet we did not know that we were truly Buddha-children.

"Now we truly understand that the World-Honored One harbors no stinginess regarding the Buddha-wisdom. Why? We have truly been Buddha-children since ancient times, yet have only delighted in the small dharma. If we had harbored the heart of delight in the great, the Buddha would have taught us the great-vehicle dharma. In this sutra, only the One Vehicle is taught, whereas in the past, before the Bodhisattvas, we disparaged the voice-hearers who delight in the small dharma. Yet the Buddha truly employed the great vehicle to teach and transform us. Therefore, we say: We originally harbored no aspiration to seek anything. Now the precious dharma-king treasure has naturally come to us, and all that Buddha-children should obtain, we have already obtained."[^8]

---

## The Disciples' Verse of Understanding

Then Mahākāśyapa, wishing to restate this meaning, spoke in verse. The extended verse parallels and reinforces the parable in poetic form, concluding with key affirmations:

*We hear the Buddha's teaching,*  
*With joy and delight exceeding measure,*  
*We have obtained what was never before obtained.*  
*The Buddha proclaimed that voice-hearers,*  
*Shall become Buddhas,*  
*And the supreme treasure-gathering,*  
*Comes without seeking.*  

*Like a young child in ignorance,*  
*Who abandoned his father and fled,*  
*Traveling through many lands for fifty years,*  
*His father grieved and sought him,*  
*But the search grew weary.*  
*He finally settled in a city,*  
*Built a house, and enjoyed the five desires.*  

*His household was extremely wealthy,*  
*With abundant gold and silver,*  
*Beryl and agate, pearls and lapis lazuli,*  
*Elephants and horses, carriages and carts,*  
*And countless fields, servants, and people.*  
*His wealth flowed out and circulated to other lands,*  
*And merchants and traders were everywhere.*  

*Thousands of millions of people,*  
*Surrounded and respected him,*  
*Always beloved of rulers,*  
*And honored by ministers and nobles.*  
*Because of various causes,*  
*Many came and went.*  
*So wealthy and powerful was he!*  
*Yet advancing in years, his concern for his son deepened.*  

*Morning and night he contemplated:*  
*'My death approaches,*  
*My foolish son abandoned me fifty years ago—*  
*What shall become of my storehouses and wealth?'*  

*At that time the poor son,*  
*Seeking food and clothing,*  
*Traveled from village to village and land to land,*  
*Sometimes finding something, sometimes finding nothing,*  
*Thin and emaciated, his body afflicted with sores.*  
*Gradually traveling, he arrived at his father's city,*  
*Hiring himself out to various employers.*  

*The wealthy householder,*  
*Was seated at his gate in magnificence,*  
*With a precious canopy above,*  
*Surrounded by his retinue,*  
*With attendants protecting him,*  
*Officials accounting for gold and silver treasures,*  
*Managing the properties and keeping records.*  

*The poor son, seeing his father's*  
*Great wealth and dignity,*  
*Thought he was a king or the equal of a king,*  
*And was startled and amazed.*  
*He thought to himself:*  
*'If I remain here long,*  
*I might be compelled and forced to work.'*  

*Thinking this, he rushed away.*  
*He asked for a poor village,*  
*Wishing to go there to work.*  
*The householder at that time,*  
*Seated at his gate,*  
*Observed his son from a distance,*  
*Recognized him and knew who he was,*  
*And immediately ordered his servants to pursue and bring him back.*  

*The poor son cried out in alarm,*  
*Confused and dizzy, collapsed on the ground.*  
*The man seizes me—I shall surely be killed!*  
*What use have I for food and clothing,*  
*That he brings me to this place?'*  
*The householder understood his son was foolish and narrow-minded,*  
*Unbelieving of his words,*  
*Unable to believe he was the father.*  

*Then employing skilful means,*  
*He sent another person,*  
*One-eyed, short and ugly, without prestige or power,*  
*To say to the poor son:*  
*'We shall hire you,*  
*And double your wages to remove the filth.*  
*The poor son heard this and joyfully followed,*  
*Working to remove filth,*  
*Cleaning the rooms.*  

*The householder looked through the window,*  
*Constantly seeing his son,*  
*Thinking his son was foolish and base,*  
*And enjoyed degrading work.*  
*So the householder,*  
*Wore worn and dirty clothes,*  
*And carried the excrement-removal tool,*  
*Going to his son's place,*  
*Employing skilful means to draw near,*  
*And speaking to him, urging him to work diligently.*  

*'I shall increase your wages,*  
*Provide oil for your feet,*  
*Supply abundant food,*  
*Offer warm bedding.*  
*I speak harshly to you:*  
*Work diligently!*  
*But I also speak gently to you:*  
*Be as my son!'*  

*The householder, being wise,*  
*Gradually caused his son to come and go,*  
*For twenty years executing household tasks,*  
*Showing him his gold, silver, pearls, and glass,*  
*All the treasures going in and out—*  
*He made him know them all.*  

*Yet still dwelling outside the gate,*  
*Living in a grass hut,*  
*Thinking of his poverty,*  
*Believing himself to lack these things.*  
*The father understood his son's mind*  
*Had gradually grown broader,*  
*Wishing to give him the wealth.*  
*He gathered his relatives, the king, great ministers, and nobles.*  

*In this great assembly,*  
*He declared:*  
*'This is my son,*  
*Who abandoned me and fled.*  
*For fifty years he has been lost.*  
*I myself came upon him and recognized him*  
*Twenty years ago.*  
*Lost in a certain city,*  
*Seeking for him, I came here.*  
*All I possess—my house and people,*  
*All is entrusted to him.*  
*He may use it as he wishes.'*  

*His son, recalling his former poverty,*  
*With his inferior aspiration,*  
*Now at his father's place*  
*Obtains precious treasures—*  
*Both the house and all the wealth,*  
*And he is extremely joyful,*  
*Having obtained what he never expected.*  

*The Buddha is likewise!*  
*Knowing we delight in the small,*  
*Never saying: 'You shall become Buddhas,'*  
*But saying we have obtained*  
*The unconditioned and perfected the small vehicle,*  
*The voice-hearer disciples.*  

*The Buddha commanded us to teach*  
*The supreme path,*  
*And those cultivating this*  
*Shall become Buddhas.*  
*We, accepting the Buddha's teaching,*  
*Taught all the Bodhisattvas*  
*Through various causes and conditions,*  
*Many parables and different expressions,*  
*The supreme path.*  

*The Buddha-children,*  
*Hearing the dharma from us,*  
*Day and night contemplating and diligently cultivating,*  
*At that time all the Buddhas*  
*Granted them prophecy:*  
*'In the future you shall become Buddhas.'*  

*All the secret dharmas of all Buddhas,*  
*We proclaimed for the Bodhisattvas alone,*  
*Their true reality.*  
*Yet not for us*  
*Did they reveal this supreme truth.*  

*Like that poor son*  
*Drawing near his father,*  
*Though knowing all the objects,*  
*His heart harbored no hope of obtaining them—*  
*We likewise, speaking of*  
*The Buddha's dharma treasures,*  
*Ourselves lacked aspiring hearts.*  

*We internally realized extinction,*  
*Believing ourselves satisfied,*  
*Understanding only this,*  
*With nothing beyond.*  
*When we heard of*  
*Pure Buddha-lands,*  
*Teaching and perfecting beings,*  
*We felt no joy whatsoever.*  

*Why? All dharmas,*  
*Are entirely empty and quiescent,*  
*Without arising or ceasing,*  
*Without great or small,*  
*Without outflows or conditioning—*  
*Contemplating thus,*  
*We generated no joy.*  

*For a long time our hearts,*  
*Had no greed or attachment,*  
*Regarding Buddha-wisdom,*  
*We had lost all aspiration.*  
*We regarded the dharma we cultivated,*  
*As ultimate.*  
*For countless nights,*  
*We cultivated the dharma of emptiness,*  
*Escaping from the three-world suffering,*  
*Dwelling in the final body,*  
*And obtaining residual nirvāṇa.*  

*The Buddha's teaching was not in vain,*  
*And we obtained the path—*  
*This is already repaying,*  
*The Buddha's kindness.*  
*Though we taught*  
*All the Buddha-children,*  
*The Bodhisattva dharma,*  
*Seeking the Buddha path,*  
*Regarding this dharma,*  
*We lacked heartfelt aspiration.*  

*The Compassionate Guide,*  
*Observing our hearts,*  
*Did not initially encourage us,*  
*Speaking of actual benefit.*  
*Like the wealthy householder,*  
*Knowing his son's inferior capacity,*  
*Through the power of skilful means,*  
*Gently subdued his heart,*  
*And then entrusted him*  
*With all the wealth.*  

*The Buddha likewise,*  
*Manifesting rare phenomena,*  
*Knowing those who delight in the small,*  
*Through the power of skilful means,*  
*Trained and subdued our hearts,*  
*And then taught the great wisdom.*  
*We now have obtained what never was,*  
*Beyond what we originally sought—*  
*Like the poor son,*  
*Obtaining measureless wealth.*  

*World-Honored One,*  
*We have obtained the path and the fruit,*  
*Regarding the unconditioned dharma,*  
*We have attained the pure eye.*  
*Through countless nights,*  
*We have upheld the Buddha's pure precepts,*  
*And only now*  
*Obtain their fruition.*  

*In the Dharma-King's domain,*  
*Long cultivating the Brahmin conduct,*  
*We now attain the unconditioned,*  
*The supreme, great fruit.*  
*We are now truly voice-hearers,*  
*Proclaiming the Buddha-path voice*  
*To all the world.*  

*We are truly Arhats now,*  
*In all the worlds—*  
*Among devas, humans, Māra, and Brahmā,*  
*Everywhere worthy of worship.*  
*The World-Honored One's tremendous kindness,*  
*With rare compassion, teaching and transforming us,*  
*Benefiting us—*  
*Who could repay such kindness?*  

*Not even by offering,*  
*Through countless eons,*  
*Could we repay,*  
*The Buddha's grace.*  

*[End of Fascicle 2 (卷二)]*

---

# Chapter 5: The Medicinal Herbs Parable

---

## The Buddha's Address to Mahākāśyapa

> "Excellent! Excellent, Mahākāśyapa. You have spoken well of the Tathāgata's true virtues. Indeed, the Tathāgata possesses measureless, inconceivable merits that could not be exhausted even if explained throughout countless kalpas. Mahākāśyapa, understand this: the Tathāgata is the sovereign of all dharmas. Whatever the Tathāgata proclaims is never false. Through wisdom and skillful means, the Tathāgata expounds all dharmas, and every dharma taught reaches the domain of all-knowing wisdom."[^1]

---

## The Medicinal Herbs Parable: The Central Metaphor

> "Mahākāśyapa, consider this: In the entire threefold thousand-fold great thousand-world system, with all its mountains, valleys, and streams, there grow medicinal herbs, forests, and shrubs of countless kinds—each with its own form, name, and character. Then a great cloud arises, dense and voluminous, spreading and enveloping the entire threefold thousand-fold great thousand-world system. At a single moment, rain pours down equally, and its waters saturate everywhere."[^4]

> "Now, in that region grow medicinal herbs—small roots, small stems, small branches, small leaves; medium roots, medium stems, medium branches, medium leaves; large roots, large stems, large branches, large leaves. Trees of various sizes stand similarly ranked by upper, middle, and lower grades. Each receives precisely according to its species-nature. Although all are nourished by a single rain, each grass and tree develops distinctly according to its own kind."[^4]

---

## Application: The Buddha as the Great Cloud

> "Mahākāśyapa, the Tathāgata appears in the world precisely as this great cloud appears. Like that great cloud spreading over the threefold thousand-fold great thousand-world system, the Tathāgata speaks with a great voice, proclaiming to all gods, humans, and asuras:

> 'I am the Tathāgata, the Worthy One, the Perfectly Enlightened One, the One Possessed of Luminous Conduct, the Well-Gone One, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassed One, the Tamer of Beings, the Teacher of Gods and Humans, the Buddha, the World-Honored One. Those not yet liberated I liberate. Those not yet awakened I awaken. Those not yet at peace I establish in peace. Those not yet entered into nirvana I guide to nirvana. I know both the present and the future as they truly are. I am the All-Knower, the All-Seer, the Knower of the Path, the Revealer of the Path, the Proclaimer of the Path. Come, all gods, humans, and asuras! Listen to the dharma.'"[^1]

---

## The Reception According to Capacity

> "At that time, countless millions of billions of beings come to the Buddha to hear the dharma. The Tathāgata observes these beings' faculties—whether sharp or dull, diligent or lazy—and teaches accordingly, in countless different ways, bringing all to joy and securing benefits for them.[^4]

> "These beings, hearing the dharma, find peace and happiness in the present life and are born in favorable realms in future lives. Through the path, they gain joy and continued opportunity to hear the dharma. Having heard it, they abandon obstacles and, to the extent of their ability, gradually enter the path. Thus, like that great cloud raining upon all forests and herbs and medicinal plants, with each receiving moisture according to its nature and becoming fully nourished and growing—so the Tathāgata's dharma rain causes all beings to grow."[^5]

---

## The One-Taste Teaching and Differential Realization

> "The Tathāgata's teaching has a single taste and aspect—namely, the taste of liberation, non-duality, and cessation, culminating in all-knowing wisdom. It ultimately reaches the realm of emptiness. Among beings who hear the Tathāgata's teaching, whether they uphold and recite it, read it, or practice it as taught, the merit they obtain is beyond their own awareness.[^7]

> "Why is this? Because only the Tathāgata knows the specific nature of each being—what it thinks upon, what it deliberates, in what manner it practices, through which dharma it thinks, deliberates, and practices, and through which dharma it attains which dharma. Beings dwell in countless different stations, and only the Tathāgata, seeing them as they truly are, perceives this without obstruction.[^8]

> "Just as medicinal herbs and trees do not themselves know whether their nature is superior, inferior, or middling, even so, the Tathāgata knows the single-taste dharma—the taste of liberation, non-duality, cessation, nirvana, the peace of final quiescence, ultimately reaching emptiness. Knowing this, the Tathāgata observes beings' desires and needs and protects them accordingly. Therefore, the Tathāgata does not immediately teach all-knowing wisdom to all beings."[^15]

---

## The Assembly's Recognition

> "Mahākāśyapa, you are rare and precious! You comprehend how the Tathāgata adapts teaching to circumstance. You are able to believe and accept it. Why? Because it is exceedingly difficult to understand and know the adaptive teachings of the Buddhas of the world."[^7]

---
## Extensive Elaboration

"The Dharma King who breaks all fixed views  
Appears within the world,  
And in accord with beings' nature  
Speaks the dharma in countless ways.  

The Honored Tathāgata,  
With wisdom deep and far-reaching,  
Long contemplates this essential truth,  
Yet has no haste to speak.  

Should one of wisdom hear it,  
One can believe and comprehend,  
But one without wisdom wavers in doubt  
And loses it forever.  

Thus, Mahākāśyapa, I teach according to capacity,  
Using countless causes and conditions  
To guide all beings to correct vision.  

Mahākāśyapa, understand this:  
It is like a great cloud  
Arising in the world,  
Enveloping all things.  

The cloud of wisdom rains moisture,  
With lightning flashing and illuminating,  
Thunder sounding far and wide,  
Delighting all beings.  

The sun is concealed and hidden,  
The earth grows cool,  
Dark clouds descend and spread  
As if they might be grasped.  

The rain pours equally in all directions,  
Flowing down on all four sides,  
With currents of water without limit,  
Saturating the entire land.  

Mountain peaks and steep valleys,  
Dark and hidden places where plants grow—  
All grasses, medicinal herbs,  
And trees both small and great,  

Grains and seedlings,  
Sugar cane and grape vines,  
All watered by the rain,  
None lacking abundance.  

The parched earth becomes saturated,  
And medicinal herbs flourish together.  
The water that comes from the clouds is of one taste,  
Yet grasses and forests receive nourishment according to their share.  

All trees, whether great or small,  
Whether upper, middle, or lower—  
Receive exactly in accordance with their size,  
Each achieving growth.  

Roots, stems, branches, and leaves,  
Flowers, fruits, and brilliant colors,  
All reached by the single rain,  
All becoming fresh and lustered.  

According to their form and nature,  
And their capacity,  
Though the moisture is one,  
Each flourishes according to its kind.  

---

## The Buddha as the Great Cloud: Continued Application

"Thus the Buddha appears in the world,  
Like a great cloud,  
Universally covering all things.  

Having appeared in the world,  
The Buddha distinguishes and proclaims  
The reality of all dharmas.  

The Great Sage and World-Honored One  
Pronounces to all gods and humans:  
'I am the Tathāgata,  
The Two-Footed One, Most Worthy,  

Appearing in the world  
Like a great cloud,  
Saturating all beings,  
The withered and parched multitudes,  

All are caused to leave suffering  
And gain peace and happiness—  
The world's happiness and also  
The happiness of nirvana.'  

All gods and human multitudes,  
Listen with one-pointed minds!  
All should come here  
To pay homage to the Unsurpassed Sage!  

I am the World-Honored One,  
Without peer or equal.  
I establish all beings in peace,  
Therefore I appear in the world.  

To the great assembly I proclaim  
The ambrosial dharma, purified.  
This dharma is of one taste:  
Liberation and nirvana!  

With one wondrous voice  
I expound this meaning,  
Always serving as a cause  
For the Great Vehicle.  

"I regard all things as equal,  
Without differences or preferences,  
Without attachment or limitation,  
Ever teaching equally to all,  

As if there were but one person,  
So too the many—thus I teach.  
Ever proclaiming the dharma,  
Never weary or abandoned,  

Standing, sitting, or moving,  
Never becoming tired,  
Fulfilling the world,  
Like rain saturating universally.  

Noble and base, superior and inferior,  
Those observing precepts and those transgressing,  
Those with perfect conduct and those without,  
Those of right view and wrong view,  

Sharp-faculty and dull-faculty beings—  
The dharma-rain falls on all equally,  
Without weariness or laziness.  

All beings who hear my dharma  
Receive according to their power,  
And dwell in various stations."  

---

## The Differentiation of Recipients: The Taxonomy of Beings

"Some dwell in human realms and celestial realms,  
Become monarchs of the turning wheel,  
Or kings among gods and Brahmā—  
These are small medicinal herbs.  

Those who know the dharma of no outflows,  
Able to attain nirvana,  
Who manifest the six supernatural powers  
And gain the three knowledges,  

Living alone in mountain forests,  
Ever practicing meditation,  
Attaining the realization of pratyekabuddhas—  
These are middle-sized medicinal herbs.  

Those seeking the World-Honored One's station,  
'I shall become a Buddha,'  
Practicing diligent meditation—  
These are the superior medicinal herbs.  

"Moreover, all Buddha-children,  
Single-heartedly devoted to the Buddha-path,  
Ever practicing compassion and loving-kindness,  
Know surely that they will become Buddhas,  

Abiding in supernatural powers,  
With unretrogressing wheels,  
Liberating countless billions  
Of hundreds of thousands of beings—  
Such Bodhisattvas are called great trees.  

"Śrāvakas and Pratyekabuddhas,  
Dwelling in mountain forests,  
In their final body,  
Hearing the dharma attain the fruit—  
These are named medicinal herbs,  
Each attaining increase.  

Should there be Bodhisattvas  
Whose wisdom is firmly established,  
Who understand all three realms,  
Seeking the highest vehicle—  
These are named small trees,  
Achieving increase.  

Moreover, those dwelling in meditation,  
Who attain supernatural powers,  
Hearing that all dharmas are empty,  
Whose minds greatly rejoice,  
Who emit light without measure,  
Liberating multitudes of beings—  
These are named great trees,  
Achieving increase.  

Thus, Mahākāśyapa,  
The dharma the Buddha proclaims  
Is like a great cloud,  
With a single-taste rain  
Moistening beings' flowers,  
Causing each to be fully realized."  

---

### Appendix: The Sanskrit Parables (Missing from Kumārajīva's Chinese)

*Note: The following section appears in the Sanskrit 'Saddharmapuṇḍarīka' (GRETIL 5.45–5.83) and other Chinese translations but is absent from Kumārajīva's Version (Taishō 262). It contains the Parable of the Blind Man and the Parable of the Potter, further illustrating the single flavor of the Dharma.*

**The Parable of the Blind Man**

"It is like a man born blind who says: 'There are no shapes, no colors, no sun, no moon, no stars. There is no one who sees them.' A physician, seeing this, gathers medicinal herbs from the snowy mountains—herbs of all four flavors. He chews them and applies them to the blind man's eyes. Suddenly, the man sees forms, colors, the sun, and the moon. He is amazed and says: 'I was foolish! I claimed these things did not exist, but now I see them all!'

"Yet, seeing the physical world, he becomes arrogant, thinking he knows everything. Then, wise seers (Rishis) with divine vision tell him: 'You have only regained physical sight. You do not yet see through walls, nor do you see the thoughts of others, nor the karma of past lives. How can you be proud?'

"Ashamed, the man seeks the path of the seers, cultivates meditation, and eventually attains true spiritual vision. The Buddha says: 'I am like that physician. The herbs are the teachings of Emptiness and Nirvana. You, the Śrāvakas, are like the blind man cured of blindness—you have cut off the afflictions of samsara, and you think this is the end. But I tell you: this is only the first stage. You have not yet attained the true Buddha-eye of omniscience. Do not be satisfied! Seek the supreme wisdom of the Tathāgata!'"

**The Parable of the Potter**

"Or consider a potter who takes the same clay and fashions different vessels—some for sugar, some for ghee, some for milk, and some for filth. The clay is one, but the vessels are defined by what they hold. Even so, the Dharma is one taste—the taste of liberation—but beings are different vessels. Some hold the vehicle of the Disciples, some the vehicle of the Pratyekabuddhas, and some the Great Vehicle of the Bodhisattvas. But ultimately, there is only the One Buddha Vehicle, designed to lead all to the state of Omniscience."

---

- Teaching reaches celestial beings and hell-dwellers, masters and servants, scholars and fools
- The appearance of difference is not contradiction of equality but expression of principle meeting conditions
- The Tathāgata never abandons any being; the dharma is accessible to all
- All beings possess Buddha-nature; ultimate enlightenment is inevitable for all

**Footnotes with Extended Analysis:**

21.**Metaphysical Implication [Eternal Recurrence]**: This connects to the eternal Buddha-principle presented in Chapter 16 (Eternally Dwelling Buddha). The ultimate point is not that beings keep reincarnating until they become Bodhisattvas, but that the Buddha's enlightenment is eternal and omnipresent, continuously teaching all beings—and all beings are continuously becoming enlightened within this eternal moment.

# Chapter 6: Prophecies

---

## The Buddha's Prophecy to Mahākāśyapa: First Among the Great Disciples

> "When I have spoken this verse, I address all the assembly and proclaim as follows: 'My disciple Mahākāśyapa, in the future world, shall gain the opportunity to pay reverence to three hundred million billion Buddhas of the World-Honored kind, making offerings to them, honoring them, respecting them, and praising them. He shall extensively proclaim the immeasurable great dharma of the Buddhas. In his final existence, he shall become a Buddha, named Radiant Light Tathāgata, Worthy One, Perfectly Enlightened One, Possessor of Luminous Conduct, Well-Gone One, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.'[^2]

> 'His land shall be called Radiant Virtue. His kalpa shall be named Great Adornment. The Buddha shall dwell for twelve small kalpas. The Proper Dharma shall dwell in the world for twenty small kalpas. The Dharma-Semblance shall also dwell for twenty small kalpas. His Buddha-realm shall be pure and adorned, without all filth, rubble, thorns, or excrement. The land shall be level, without any hills or depressions. Lapis lazuli shall be its soil. Precious trees shall stand in rows along the paths, marked by golden cords. Precious flowers shall be scattered everywhere, and the entire realm shall be perfectly pure.'[^3]

> 'In that Buddha-land, there shall be immeasurable thousands of billions of Bodhisattvas, and the assembly of śrāvakas shall likewise be numberless. There shall be no demonic interference. Though demons and demonic beings exist, they all protect the Buddha-dharma.'"[^4]

---

## Verse Elaboration: Mahākāśyapa's Future Path

*"Listen, all monks, I declare to you*  
*With the Buddha-eye, I see Mahākāśyapa.*  
*In the future world, after countless kalpas,*  
*He shall become a Buddha.*  

*In that future age, he shall encounter*  
*Three hundred million billion Buddhas,*  
*Making supreme offerings with pure conduct,*  
*For the attainment of Buddha-wisdom.*  

*Having offered to the Unsurpassed, Two-Footed One,*  
*And cultivated all supreme wisdom,*  
*In his final existence, he shall become a Buddha.*  

*"His Buddha-land shall be supremely pure,*  
*Lapis lazuli shall be its soil,*  
*Many precious trees adorning it,*  
*Lined with golden cords marking the paths.*  

*Ever exhaling wondrous fragrance,*  
*Scattered with countless flowers and petals,*  
*Adorned with varied marvelous things.*  

---

## The Other Great Disciples' Request: Recognition of Their Yearning

*"His land shall be perfectly level,*  
*With no hills or depressions.*  
*The assembly of Bodhisattvas shall be incalculable,*  
*Their hearts gentle and flexible,*  
*Endowed with great supernatural powers,*  
*Upholding the Buddha's Great Vehicle teachings.*  

*The assembly of śrāvakas shall have no outflows,*  
*"Listen, all monks, I declare to you*
*With the Buddha-eye, I see Mahākāśyapa.*
*In the future world, after countless kalpas,*
*He shall become a Buddha.*

*In that future age, he shall encounter*
*Three hundred million billion Buddhas,*
*Making supreme offerings with pure conduct,*
*For the attainment of Buddha-wisdom.*

*Having offered to the Unsurpassed, Two-Footed One,*
*And cultivated all supreme wisdom,*
*In his final existence, he shall become a Buddha.*

*"His Buddha-land shall be supremely pure,*
*Lapis lazuli shall be its soil,*
*Many precious trees adorning it,*
*Lined with golden cords marking the paths.*

*Ever exhaling wondrous fragrance,*
*Scattered with countless flowers and petals,*
*Adorned with varied marvelous things.*

*"His land shall be perfectly level,*
*With no hills or depressions.*
*The assembly of Bodhisattvas shall be incalculable,*
*Their hearts gentle and flexible,*
*Endowed with great supernatural powers,*
*Upholding the Buddha's Great Vehicle teachings.*

*The assembly of śrāvakas shall have no outflows,*
*Dwelling in their final existence,*
*As sons of the Dharma-King,*
*Also incalculable,*
*Even the celestial eye cannot count their number.*

*"The Buddha's lifespan shall be twelve small kalpas.*
*The Proper Dharma shall dwell in the world twenty small kalpas.*
*The Dharma-Semblance shall likewise dwell twenty small kalpas.*
*The World-Honored One Radiant Light shall be thus."*

> "At that moment, the Great Maudgalyāyana, Subodhi, and Mahākātyāyana were all stirred with emotion. They trembled, clasped their hands in unison, gazed upon the World-Honored One's face without turning away their eyes, and together spoke in verse:"[^11]

## The Disciples' Supplication: Verse of Yearning

*"Great, Mighty World-Honored One,*  
*Dharma-King of all the Shakya clan,*  
*For our sake, you have bestowed Buddha's voice.*  
*If you know our deepest hearts*  
*And see fit to grant us prophecy,*  

*It would be as if pouring cooling nectar*  
*Upon beings burning with great heat.*  
*We are like one who comes from a land of famine*  
*And suddenly encounters the great king's meal.*  
*Though the heart still harbors fear and doubt,*  
*Not yet daring to take the food immediately.*  

*But if the king gives instruction,*  
*Then one may eat without hesitation.*  
*So we too, though hearing Buddha's word*  
*That we shall become Buddhas,*  
*Still harbor anxiety and fear,*  
*Like one not yet daring to eat.*  

*But if you grant us prophecy,*  
*Then all will be comfort and joy.*  
*Great, Mighty World-Honored One,*  
*Forever wishing to establish peace in the world,*  
*We beseech you: grant us the prophecy*  
*As the famished require your instruction!"*[^13]  

---

## The Buddha's Recognition of Subodhi: "Wisdom" Becomes Buddha

> "At that time, the World-Honored One, knowing the minds of the great disciples, told the assembly of monks: 'This Subodbi, in the future world, shall pay reverence to three hundred million billion nayutas of Buddhas, making offerings and honoring them with reverence and respect. After the final nirvana of each of these Buddhas, in each case he shall erect stupas, great stupas, each a thousand yojanas high, stretching and extending one hundred yojanas in each direction. Each shall be composed of gold, silver, lapis lazuli, crystal, pearl, carnelian, and the seven precious substances. Above them shall be hung jeweled garlands, countless flowers, incense, powdered incense, burning incense, silk canopies, and banners, all for making offerings to the stupas.'[^16]

> 'Beyond this, he shall pay reverence to twenty billion billion Buddhas, in the same manner. Having completed his offerings to all these Buddhas, he shall perfect the Bodhisattva path and become a Buddha, called "Renowned by Name" Tathāgata, Worthy One, Perfectly Enlightened One, Possessor of Luminous Conduct, Well-Gone One, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.'[^17]

> 'In his Buddha-realm, the land shall be perfectly pure, composed of lapis lazuli, with precious trees adorning it, marked by golden cords along the paths. The ground shall be level, without any hills or depressions. The Bodhisattvas shall be countless, all possessing sharp faculties, their wheels never retreating. His Buddha-land shall be adorned with Bodhisattvas. The assembly of śrāvakas shall be immeasurable, all possessing the three knowledges and the six supernatural powers, dwelling in the eight liberations, possessing great majesty.'[^18]

> 'The Buddha's proclamation of dharma shall manifest in countless supernatural transformations beyond all conception. Gods and humans, numberless as the sand of the Ganges, shall all join palms in unison, listening to the Buddha's words. The Buddha shall dwell for twelve small kalpas. The Proper Dharma shall dwell in the world for twenty small kalpas, and the Dharma-Semblance shall likewise dwell for twenty small kalpas.'"[^19]

---

## Verse Elaboration for Subodhi

*"Listen, all monks assembled here.*  
*Attend with one heart to what I proclaim.*  
*My great disciple Subodhi*  
*Shall certainly become a Buddha,*  
*Named 'Renowned by Name.'*  

*He shall encounter numberless Buddha-billions,*  
*Following the path each Buddha walks,*  
*Gradually perfecting the Great Way.*  

*In his final existence,*  
*He shall attain the thirty-two marks,*  
*Splendid and beautiful,*  
*Like a jeweled mountain.*  

---

## The Prophecy to Mahākātyāyana: Analysis into Wisdom

> "The World-Honored One again told the assembly of monks: 'I declare to you that the great Mahākātyāyana, in the future age, shall make offerings and honor eight thousand billion Buddhas with all manner of precious offerings. After each Buddha enters final nirvana, he shall erect great stupas, each a thousand yojanas high, spreading one hundred yojanas in each direction, composed of gold, silver, lapis lazuli, crystal, pearl, carnelian, rose-quartz, and the seven precious substances. All shall be adorned with countless flowers, garlands, fragrant powders and pastes, burning incense, silk canopies, and banners, making offerings to the stupas.'[^23]

> 'Beyond this, he shall make offerings to twenty billion billion Buddhas, likewise. Having offered to all these Buddhas and perfected the Bodhisattva path, he shall become a Buddha, named "Jambu-Gold-Radiance" Tathāgata, Worthy One, Perfectly Enlightened One, Possessor of Luminous Conduct, Well-Gone One, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.'[^24]

> 'His Buddha-realm shall be perfectly pure, composed of lapis lazuli, adorned with precious trees, with golden cords marking the paths. His land shall be level, without hills or depressions. There shall be no realm of hell-beings, hungry ghosts, animals, or asuras. There shall be many gods and humans, Bodhisattvas and śrāvakas, adorning his Buddha-land. The Buddha shall dwell for twelve small kalpas. The Proper Dharma shall dwell for twenty small kalpas, and the Dharma-Semblance likewise for twenty small kalpas.'"[^25]

---

## Verse for Mahākātyāyana

*"Listen, all monks assembled here.*  
*Believe what I proclaim as true.*  
*This Mahākātyāyana*  
*Shall offer precious goods and supplies*  
*To pay reverence to many Buddhas.*  

*After the Buddhas have passed into nirvana,*  
*He shall erect seven-jeweled stupas,*  
*Tall banners and golden spires,*  
*With flowers and incense making offerings*  
*To the Buddha-stupas.*  

*In his final existence,*  
*He shall attain Buddha-wisdom,*  
*And realize perfect enlightenment.*  

*"His Buddha-land shall be pure,*  
*He shall liberate immeasurable, billion multitudes.*  
*All ten directions shall make offerings to him,*  
*And the Buddha's luminosity shall be unsurpassed.*  
*His Buddha shall be named Gold-Radiance.*  

*Bodhisattvas and śrāvakas,*  
*Having severed all remaining attachments,*  
*Shall be numberless,*  
*Adorning his Buddha-land.*  

---

## The Prophecy to Maudgalyāyana: Supernatural Power as Compassion

> "The World-Honored One again told the great assembly: 'I declare to you that the great Maudgalyāyana, in the future age, shall offer with precious supplies and honor eight thousand Buddhas with respect and reverence. After each Buddha enters final nirvana, he shall erect great stupas, each a thousand yojanas high, spreading five hundred yojanas in each direction, all composed of gold, silver, lapis lazuli, crystal, pearl, carnelian, rose-quartz, and the seven precious substances. All shall be adorned with countless flowers, garlands, fragrant incenses, silk canopies, and banners, making offerings to the stupas. Beyond this, he shall make offerings to two hundred billion billion Buddhas in the same manner.'[^27]

> 'Having made complete offerings to all these Buddhas, he shall become a Buddha, named "Tamalapatri, Sandalwood-Fragrance" Tathāgata, Worthy One, Perfectly Enlightened One, Possessor of Luminous Conduct, Well-Gone One, Knower of the World, Unsurpassed One, Tamer of Beings, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One.'[^28]

> 'His Buddha-realm shall be in the kalpa called "Joy-Fulfillment," in the land called "Joyful Intention." The land shall be perfectly level, composed of lapis lazuli, adorned with precious trees, scattered with true pearls and flowers, perfectly pure and beautiful to behold. There shall be many gods and humans, Bodhisattvas and śrāvakas, adorning that Buddha-land. The Buddha's lifespan shall be twenty-four small kalpas—twice that of the other Buddhas just named.'[^29]

> 'The Proper Dharma shall dwell in the world for forty small kalpas, and the Dharma-Semblance shall likewise dwell for forty small kalpas.'"[^30]

---

## Verse for Maudgalyāyana

*"My disciple, the great Maudgalyāyana,*  
*Having discarded this body,*  
*Shall encounter eight thousand,*  
*Two hundred billion Buddhas,*  

*For the Buddha-path's sake,*  
*Honoring them with reverence.*  

*In all Buddha-realms,*  
*He shall ever practice the pure conduct,*  
*Throughout measureless kalpas,*  
*Maintaining the Buddha-dharma.*  

*After the Buddhas have passed into nirvana,*  
*He shall erect seven-jeweled stupas,*  
*With tall golden banners and spires,*  
*Flowers, incense, and music*  
*Offered to the Buddha-stupas.*  

*Gradually completing the Bodhisattva path,*  
*In the land of Joy-Intention,*  
*He shall become a Buddha,*  
*Named Tamalapatri, Sandalwood-Fragrance.*  

*"That Buddha's lifespan*  
*Shall be twenty-four kalpas.*  
*Always shall he teach the Buddha-way*  
*To gods and humans.*  

*Śrāvakas without limit,*  
*Like the sand of the Ganges,*  
*Possessing the three knowledges and six powers,*  
*Dwelling in great majesty.*  

*Bodhisattvas without number,*  
*With firm resolve and diligent practice,*  
*Never retreating from Buddha-wisdom.*  

*After the Buddha has passed into nirvana,*  
*The Proper Dharma shall endure*  
*For forty small kalpas,*  
*And the Dharma-Semblance likewise."*  

---

## The Promise of Five Hundred Prophecies

> "My disciples, possessing complete majesty—there are five hundred of you—all shall receive prophecy granting them Buddhahood in future ages.
