"**From that time until now, I have always remained in this Sahā world, teaching and transforming sentient beings. Furthermore, I have also in other places—in hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of *asamkhyeya* worlds—guided and benefited living beings.**[^8]

---
## THE REVELATION OF SKILLFUL MEANS: APPARENT EXTINCTION
"Good children, during this immeasurable span,**I have spoken of other Buddhas—such as the Buddha Dipamkara and others—proclaiming that they had entered into *parinirvāṇa*. Yet all of this was skillful teaching, skillful explanation. In truth, these were all adaptations to beings' capacities.**[^9]

"When sentient beings come to me,**I observe with my Buddha-eye their faith, their spiritual faculties, their dullness or sharpness of perception, their individual inclinations and habits. According to what they require, I manifest different names, different ages. Sometimes I proclaim my own extinction, sometimes that of others. Employing various causes and conditions, parables, and subtle teachings, I cause all sentient beings to develop joy and gladness in their hearts.**

"Good children, when the Tathāgata observes sentient beings who delight in limited teachings, who are defiled in merit and heavy with karmic obstruction, he teaches them:**'In my past life, I practiced austere discipline. Only recently have I attained supreme, perfect enlightenment.'**

"**Yet in truth, I have been a Buddha for incalculable eons. I speak in this manner only to teach sentient beings, to lead them into the Buddha-way and cause them to awaken to enlightenment.**[^10]

---
## THE PRINCIPLE: ALL TEACHING IS TRUE, NONE IS DECEPTIVE
"Good children, whatever teachings the Tathāgata reveals,**whether concerning my own body, the bodies of others, my own affairs, or the affairs of others—whether showing my own form or the forms of others—all such teachings are absolutely true and not deceptive.** Why is this?

"The Tathāgata directly perceives and knows the characteristics of the three worlds with perfect clarity:**"There is no arising, no passing away, no abiding in the world, and no extinction.**[^11]**"There is no actual and no illusory, no such-ness and no differ-ness. It is not as the limited perception within the three worlds perceives the three worlds. The Tathāgata sees these matters with perfect clarity and without error.** [^12]

"**Because all sentient beings possess different natures, different desires, different practices, different habitual tendencies, different ways of imagining and discriminating, in order to cause all to generate wholesome roots and advance toward enlightenment, through countless causes and conditions, through innumerable parables and illustrations, through various forms of teaching, the Tathāgata performs the work of teaching without ever interrupting or ceasing.**[^13]

"Thus, since I attained Buddhahood,**an immeasurably vast span of time has passed. My lifespan is measureless asankhya kalpas, constantly abiding without extinction. I have not yet entered parinirvāṇa, and my life is not yet exhausted.**[^14]

---
## THE REASON: COMPASSION THROUGH SEEMING ABSENCE
"Good children, during my previous practice of the Bodhisattva path, I perfected a lifespan through cultivation.**Even now, that lifespan has not been exhausted—in fact, it remains doubled beyond what it was.**[^15]

"**However, I do not truly disappear. Yet I announce my extinction. Why?** Because the Tathāgata perceives that**when beings of weak merit lack sufficient wholesome roots, are defiled by heavy karmic obstacles, are driven by greed toward the five desires, dwelling in delusion—if they see the Tathāgata constantly present and never departing, such beings would become arrogant and careless. They would not generate the thought: 'This opportunity to encounter the Buddha is rare and precious.' They would not develop reverence, respect, and longing toward the Buddha. They would not create wholesome roots.**[^16]

"**For this reason, the Tathāgata teaches them, saying: 'Monks and nuns, Buddhas appearing in the world are difficult and rare to encounter.'** [^17]

Why?**Because when the weaker beings, after passing through hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of *asamkhyeya* eons, can at last perceive a Buddha, or perhaps never perceive one at all—**for this reason, I teach:**'The Tathāgata is difficult to perceive and encounter.'**[^18]

"Through hearing such words, these sentient beings will certainly develop the thought:**'The meeting with a Buddha is rare and difficult. I must seize this precious opportunity.' In this way, they generate reverence and respect, cultivate longing and yearning for the Buddha, and thereby plant wholesome roots. Thus the Tathāgata, though not actually entering extinction, proclaims that he shall enter extinction.**[^19]

---
## THE PARABLE OF THE GOOD PHYSICIAN
"Furthermore, good children, this is the method of all Buddhas. It is like the case of a skillful, wise physician, intelligent and experienced, knowledgeable of medicinal treatments, capable of healing all illnesses.[^20]

"This physician has many sons—perhaps ten, perhaps twenty, perhaps a hundred in number. Due to some circumstance, he must journey to a distant country. While he is absent, all his sons drink poison by mistake. The poison has taken effect in their bodies, causing them to writhe upon the ground in agony.

"At that moment, their father returns home. Seeing his sons suffering in this way, he is moved with compassion.**The father consults many medical texts, seeking excellent herbs—herbs that are fine and beautiful in color, aroma, and taste—possessing all wholesome qualities. He gathers these herbs, grinds and mixes them, and gives them to his sons, speaking thus:****'This is truly a supremely good medicine. Its color, aroma, and taste are all complete and excellent. Consume this now, and your suffering will swiftly be relieved. You shall no longer have any disease or affliction.'**[^21]

"Among the sons, those whose minds have not been too damaged by poison, seeing this excellent medicine with its fine color and aroma, immediately accept it and consume it completely. Their illnesses are entirely cured.[^22]

"However, among the other sons, whose minds have been severely damaged by the poison and clouded in delusion—though they also see their father's arrival and joyfully greet him, begging for treatment—**when given the excellent medicine, they refuse to drink it. Why? Because the poison has deeply entered their systems, causing their minds to become confused and deluded. They regard the excellent medicine with its fine color and aroma as ugly and unpalatable.**[^23]

"The father thinks to himself:**'These sons are most pitiable. The poison has so clouded their understanding that even when offered excellent medicine, they will not accept it. I must devise a skillful method to make them take this medicine.'**

"Accordingly, he tells them:**'You must understand—I have grown old. My death is near. This excellent medicine I now leave with you. You may take it without anxiety.'** [^24]

"Having given these instructions, the father departs to a distant country, sending messengers back with word:**'Your father has died.'**[^25]

"Upon hearing that their father is deceased, the sons experience great sorrow and grief. They reflect:**'If our father were here, he would have compassion upon us and could rescue us. Now he has abandoned us, departing to a distant country and dying there.'**

"Recognizing their own abandonment and loneliness, the sons eventually awaken to wisdom. They finally examine the excellent medicine that their father left behind, finding it to be of excellent color and delightful aroma. They take it and consume it completely, and all their poisons are entirely cured.

"The father, learning that all his sons have recovered, returns immediately and meets with them, revealing himself."[^26]

---
## THE APPLICATION: THE BUDDHA AS SKILLED PHYSICIAN
"Good children, what is your opinion? Could anyone rightly accuse this excellent physician of falsehood or deception?"[^27]

The assembly responded: "No, World Honored One."

The Buddha declared: "I am exactly like this physician.**I have been a Buddha for immeasurable, incalculable hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of *asamkhyeya* eons. For the sake of sentient beings, through skillful means, I proclaim my extinction. Yet there is no one who can justly accuse the Tathāgata of falsehood or deception.** "[^28]

---
## THE ETERNAL BUDDHA
At that moment, the World Honored One, wishing to elaborate further upon this teaching, spoke in verse:

"Since I attained Buddhahood,
The eons that have passed
Are numberless and incalculable—
Hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of *asamkhyeya* eons.

Constantly I have taught and transformed
Hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of beings,
Leading them to enter the Buddha-way.
Throughout all these eons,

To liberate sentient beings,
I have employed skillful means to manifest extinction.
Yet in truth I do not disappear—
I remain constantly, teaching in this place.[^29]

I remain always in this world,
Through all supernatural powers,
Making those with inverted views—
Though dwelling near—unable to perceive me.

The multitudes perceive my extinction,
And make extensive offerings to my relics,
All harboring yearning and longing,
Generating hearts of devoted aspiration.

When sentient beings develop faith and trust,
When their minds become sincere and gentle,
With minds devoted to seeing the Buddha,
Regardless of sacrifice of body or life—

At that time, I and the assembly
Appear together at Vulture Peak,
And I address all sentient beings, saying:
'I remain constantly here, never departing—

Through skillful means I manifest
Both extinction and non-extinction.
Other lands contain sentient beings
Who are respectful and reverent believers.

For them I teach the supreme Dharma.
You do not hear this teaching,
For you believe that I have entered extinction.

I perceive all sentient beings
Sinking in the ocean of suffering.
Therefore, I do not manifest my body—
I cause them to develop longing and yearning.

Sinking in the depths of their desire,
They then awaken to seek the Buddha.
My supernatural powers are such as this—
Throughout immeasurable eons,

I remain constantly at Vulture Peak,
As well as at all other dwelling-places.
When the multitude perceives the eon-fire
Burning and destroying all worlds,

My Buddha-land remains eternally peaceful,
Filled continuously with gods and humans.
The gardens and palaces,
Various precious adornments—

Precious trees bear flowers and fruit,
And all beings dwell in joy and pleasure.
The gods strike their celestial drums,
Always performing various musical performances,

Raining down the mandāra flowers,
Scattering them upon the Buddha and the great assembly.
My pure Buddha-land is not destroyed,
Yet the multitude perceives it burned entirely.[^30]

Anxiety and terror,
And all kinds of suffering fill them.
These sinful beings,
Because of their evil karma,

Throughout immeasurable eons
Cannot even hear the name of the Three Jewels.
But those who have cultivated merit,
Who are gentle and sincere of heart,

All perceive my body present here
Teaching the Dharma.
Sometimes to this assembly I proclaim
That the Buddha's lifespan is immeasurable,

Sometimes I teach that the Buddha is rarely encountered,
Depending upon what causes beings to awaken.
My wisdom-power is like this—
The light of wisdom illuminates immeasurably.

The lifespan is incalculable eons,
Accomplished through long cultivation.
All wise ones—do not generate doubt about this,
Put an end to your doubts forever.

The Buddha's words are true and real, not false.
Just as the excellent physician,
Employing skillful means to heal his deluded sons,
Though truly present, proclaims his death—

There is no one who can call the physician false.
I am also the father of the world,
Rescuing all beings from suffering.
For those subject to delusion,

I am truly present, yet proclaim my extinction.
Though deluded beings fail to perceive,
The Buddha constantly dwells here.
With all supernatural power,

I am conscious of all sentient beings—
Who practices the path and who does not.
I adapt all teachings accordingly,
In order that all may enter supreme wisdom and

Swiftly accomplish the Buddha-body."[^31]

---

# Chapter 17: The Discrimination of Merits

## The Universal Merit of Single-Verse Rejoicing
At that time, the great assembly gathered around the Buddha heard his revelation of the Tathāgata's eternal lifespan—that immeasurable, incalculable eons have passed since he first attained enlightenment. Countless bodhisattvas, beginning with Maitreya, experienced tremendous benefit from this teaching. Incalculable aeons of beings obtained the spiritual threshold of anuttara-samyak-sambodhi; thousands of times that number of great bodhisattvas gained the dharma-gate of dhāraṇī-hearing; entire assemblies whose numbers equal the dust-particles of a world-realm achieved unrestricted eloquence in explaining dharma; other assemblies numbering like the dust-grains of three thousand great thousand-worlds obtained hundreds of millions upon millions of wheel-turning mantric formulas; three thousand world-realms' worth of great bodhisattvas turned the irreversible dharma-wheel; twice that number developed the dharma-wheel of purity; the bodhisattva-assembly of a thousand worlds would attain supreme enlightenment in eight succeeding lifetimes; four-world-realm assemblies in four lifetimes; three-world-realm assemblies in three; two-world-realm assemblies in two; one-world-realm assemblies in one remaining lifetime; eight complete world-realms' worth of beings awakened the incomparable resolve for supreme enlightenment[^1].

The Buddha declared to Maitreya: "When I spoke this teaching concerning the Tathāgata's eternal lifespan, the merit obtained by beings who developed even a single moment of authentic understanding would be immeasurable and infinite. If a virtuous man or woman devoted himself or herself to practicing the five perfections[^2]—generosity, moral discipline, patience, energetic effort, and meditation—through eighty million billion nayuta eons in order to attain incomparable supreme enlightenment, the merit obtained from such practice across all these eons would not equal one-hundredth, one-thousandth, one-hundred-thousandth, one-billion-fold of the merit gained by a single instant of authentic understanding regarding the Tathāgata's eternal lifespan. The disparity is such that even enumeration and analogy fail to capture it[^3].

"Listen further, Maitreya. When one hears of the Tathāgata's eternal lifespan and genuinely comprehends its meaning, the merit obtained is boundless, awakening the Tathāgata's incomparable wisdom. How much greater then when one extensively learns this sutra, teaches it to others, maintains it personally, or writes it out with one's own hand, embellishing it with flowers, fragrant oils, garlands, silk banners, jeweled canopies, and precious incense-vessels burning incense of immeasurable value? Such a person obtains merit of infinite and boundless measure, capable of generating the wisdom of all phenomena[^4].

"When virtuous men and women hear my proclamation of the Tathāgata's eternal lifespan and develop profound, heartfelt understanding, they directly perceive the Buddha dwelling eternally on Vulture Peak Mountain, surrounded by great bodhisattvas and assemblies of śrāvakas, explaining the dharma. Furthermore, they behold this Sahā world-realm: its ground composed of lapis lazuli, level and smooth; eight roads marked with grades of gold; rows of precious trees; towers, pavilions, and observation halls all manifested in the seven jewels; bodhisattva assemblies inhabiting these places. Whoever is able to perceive in this manner possesses marks of profound authentic understanding[^5].

"Furthermore, after the Tathāgata's passing, should one hear this sutra and neither reject nor disparage it, instead generating a mind of sincere rejoicing, understand that such a being has already established marks of profound understanding. What need be said of those who actually read it aloud, maintain it, or practice accordingly? These people carry the Tathāgata atop their heads. I declare, Maitreya, that such virtuous men and women need not establish stupas or construct monastic dwellings for the support of the sangha. Why is this? Those virtuous men and women who receive and maintain, read and recite this sutra have already established stupas, constructed monastic dwellings, and provided support to the sangha. They have, in effect, raised seven-jeweled stupas honoring the Tathāgata's relics, reaching toward the Brahma realms, hung with jeweled banners and numberless precious bells, decorated with flowers, fragrant oils, garlands, and powdered incenses. Through immeasurable thousands of millions of eons, they have continuously offered such worship[^6].

"I declare, Maitreya: after my passing, should one hear this sutra and be able to maintain it—whether by writing it themselves or arranging for it to be written—they thereby construct a monastic dwelling, a great temple of sandalwood wood with thirty-two halls, eight palm-trees high, ornate and sublime. Thousands of millions of monastics dwell therein, supplied with all necessities: gardens, bathing pools, pathways for circumambulation, meditation caves, robes, food, beds, medicinal preparations, and all requisite pleasures. Such monastic dwellings in their diversity number countless thousands of millions[^7].

"The presence of these temples constitutes worship of me and the assembly of monks. Therefore I proclaim: after my passage, should one receive and maintain, read and recite, explain and write this sutra, one has no need to establish stupas or construct monastic dwellings. Moreover, should such a person also practice charity along with discipline, patience, energetic effort, singular concentration, and transcendent wisdom—these virtues are supreme and boundless, like the infinite space extending east, west, north, south, upward, and downward—the spiritual merit becomes equally immeasurable and infinite, swiftly approaching all-knowing wisdom[^8].

"When one reads, maintains, and explains this sutra for others, or writes it, coupled with the capacity to establish stupas and construct monastic dwellings, to honor and praise assemblies of śrāvakas with myriads of praises, and to praise bodhisattva-merits with myriad commendations; when one explains this Lotus Sutra with various reasons and skilled language suited to its meaning; when one maintains pure moral discipline, dwells in harmony with gentle companions, practices patience without anger, steadies one's mind with firm intention, treasures sitting in meditation and attains deep absorptions, pursues energetic effort with heroic courage, embraces all wholesome dharmas, and possesses keen wisdom with clever answers to inquiries—if one practices in this manner, one's merit becomes incomparable. Whoever perceives such a dharma-teacher, having perfected such virtues, should scatter heavenly flowers upon that person, drape them with heavenly garments, bow with their head to the person's feet, and generate the thought-pattern of seeing a Buddha in them.

"Moreover, cultivate this thought: 'This person shall not long remain before reaching the bodhi-path, attaining the unsurpassable, non-action wisdom, and bringing benefit to countless beings of human and heavenly realms.' Wherever such a person dwells, whether standing, sitting, or walking, they are present in a place where a stupa should be erected, adorned with elaborate reverence and manifold offerings. The Buddha-child dwelling in that space represents the Buddha's own enjoyment, constantly dwelling therein in walking and sitting meditation[^9]."

## The Celebration of Merit-Discrimination

*At that time, the Tathāgata wished to restate this meaning through verse:*

When one hears this dharma in a great assembly,
And rejoices, even hearing but a single verse,
Conveying that teaching to others who then rejoice,
This happiness extending through fifty sequential recipients—
The merit of the fiftieth person who heard
A single verse with joyful heart
Shall now be differentiated and fully explained.

Consider a great benefactor who possesses
Supplies of entertainment for countless beings,
Sustaining their delight through eighty full years,
Providing whatever each creature desires,
Yet seeing their weakness in advanced age—
Hair white, faces wrinkled,
Teeth sparse, forms withered—
Recognizing that death approaches,
This benefactor thinks, "Now I must guide them
Toward the dharma and the fruit of enlightenment,"
And speaks to them the profound truth:
"All existence is impermanent as mist and foam and flame—
Each of you must develop the mind of renunciation."

All who heard this dharma teaching
Directly attained arhatship,
Possessing the six divine penetrations,
Three insights, and eight deliverances.
Yet consider: the fiftieth person
Who but heard one verse with joy—
That person's merit surpasses this great patron
Beyond the reach of any comparison.

Such retransmitted rejoicing holds merit infinite,
Immeasurable in extent—
What need be said of those who first heard and rejoiced
In the great assembly gathering?

Should someone say to another:
"A most profound sutra exists, called the Lotus—
Let us together attend its teaching!"
And that person, receiving this invitation,
Listens to the dharma, even for the briefest moment—
The fruition of such virtue shall now be explained:

In each succeeding lifetime,
The person's mouth shall bear no ailments:
Teeth neither sparse nor yellow nor black,
Lips neither thick, sunken, nor rough,
The tongue never parched, dark, nor shortened,
The nose straight, refined, and perfect,
The forehead broad, level, and well-proportioned,
The facial features harmonious and complete—
All marks of human perfection intact—
The breath never fouled by unpleasant odor,
But instead fragrant with lotus-blossom scent
Emanating from the mouth itself[^10].

When one deliberately journeys to the monastery
Wishing to hear this sutra's profound dharma,
Delighting even for a moment in such hearing—
The karmic fruition shall manifest thus:
In successive rebirths among gods and humans,
The person obtains excellent elephants, horses, conveyances,
Precious palanquins and jeweled transport,
And the celestial mansions of heavenly realms.

Should one counsel another to sit and listen
To this dharma in the place of teaching,
Sharing one's seat to allow their attendance—
Such merit produces the seat of Śakra,
The seat of Brahma the Lord,
Or the throne of the Chakravartin King.

How much greater is the merit of those
Who single-mindedly listen with one heart,
Penetrating and explaining the meaning,
Practicing exactly as the dharma instructs—
Such merit proves immeasurable[^11].

## Philosophical Implications

Chapter 17 represents a turning point in the Lotus Sutra's teaching: the transition from metaphysical revelation (the Buddha's eternal presence) to ethical consequence (the actual benefits of maintaining the dharma). The chapter operates on a principle of recursive amplification—each act of dharma transmission multiplies the merit of the previous transmission, until the fiftieth person's mere rejoicing surpasses eighty million billion eons of concentrated practice by another being.

This asymmetry reveals a profound Buddhist principle: the merit of recognizing the dharma vastly exceeds the merit of conventional practice. The virtuous man who gives sustenance for eighty years, then teaches the dharma to attain arhats, obtains immense merit. Yet a being who merely hears one verse and rejoices transcends that entire achievement. Why?

Because dharma-recognition operates on a different ontological level. Conventional merit (dana, sila, concentration) addresses suffering within cyclic existence. But recognizing the dharma addresses the structure itself. When one responds to the Lotus Sutra's teaching regarding the Buddha's eternal lifespan, one perceives the reality that cyclical existence has been transcended (from the Buddha's perspective) eons ago. This recognition is itself partial enlightenment.

The chapter also articulates a crucial Buddhist principle regarding merit transference and sequential communication. The teaching passes from Buddha to first hearer to second hearer through fiftieth hearer, with each stage potentially reducing the "original" authority. Yet the Buddha's point is exactly opposite: the fiftieth person's rejoicing contains merit equal to or exceeding earlier participants. This suggests that dharma's efficacy does not depend on proximity to source but on the authenticity of recognition. The five-hundredth hearer in a transmission chain receives dharmic truth equally vivid.

The physical benefits described (dental perfection, vocal sweetness, refined facial features) might appear mundane. But they possess profound symbolic significance in Buddhist cosmology. Such marks indicate beings capable of effective dharma-teaching. Perfect speech, attractive presence, and freedom from physical disabilities enable the dharma-teacher to function optimally. The "perfect person" described in this chapter is not beautified for vanity but equipped for efficacy.

The mantra-like repetition—"may my mouth emit lotus fragrance rather than foul odor"—reflects Buddhist understanding that consciousness and body interpenetrate. What emerges from one's mouth (speech) follows from what dwells in one's consciousness. Speech should "naturally" emanate sweetness because the mind dwells in dharma. This is not magical transformation but the natural result of consciousness-reorientation.

The chapter also pioneers a distinctive Buddhist argument: the value of "merely" hearing and rejoicing. In many traditions, spiritual practice requires extensive effort, discipline, and technique. The Lotus Sutra suggests that authentic participation in dharma-recognition requires nothing but openness. To hear the sutra and rejoice—to recognize its truth without rejection—is itself sufficient. This opens the dharma-path to beings excluded by conventional criteria (the lazy, the inept, those of limited capacity). The sutra's revolutionary inclusivity appears here not as arbitrary generosity but as theological necessity: dharma-recognition itself is universal, not restricted to yogic adepts or scholarly virtuosos.

The architectural imagery—stupas, monasteries, eight-hall temples—operates both literally and symbolically. The sutra-maintainer does not merely benefit from maintaining a text; the maintenance of the sutra itself becomes a form of worship equivalent to constructing elaborate monuments. This reflects a sophisticated Buddhist understanding that dharma and its material manifestations are not separate. The written text, carefully preserved and transmitted, is not merely a conveyance of concepts but a sacred presence itself.

# Chapter 18: The Merit of Rejoicing

## The Recursive Multiplication of Rejoicing
At that time, Maitreya Bodhisattva approached the Buddha with reverent demeanor and inquired: "World-Honored One, when beings hear this Lotus Sutra and develop a mind of sincere rejoicing, what measure of merit do they acquire?"

The Buddha replied to Maitreya: "After the Tathāgata's passing, should a bhikṣu, bhikṣuṇī, upāsaka, or upāsikā—indeed, any wise being whether elder or youth—hear this sutra and sincerely rejoice, understanding its meaning, they should proceed from that assembly to other locations: monastic dwellings, open spaces, towns, villages, highways, or fields. There they shall explain this dharma to father, mother, relatives, or knowledgeable companions, conveying what they have heard according to their understanding. These listeners, upon hearing, shall themselves generate rejoicing and communicate this joy to still others. In this manner, from person to person, the transmission continues until the fiftieth recipient is reached[^1].

"Maitreya, observe the merit of this fiftieth virtuous man or woman who has received the joy of the sutra through sequential transmission. I shall now elucidate it for you.

"Consider four hundred million billion nayuta world-realms populated with six-path beings of all types—those born from eggs, wombs, moisture, and transformation; those with form and formless; those with consciousness and unconscious; those neither conscious nor unconscious; those with no feet, two feet, four feet, or multiple feet—in essence, all categories of sentient life. Suppose a great benefactor, wishing to acquire merit, provides to each of these innumerable beings whatever objects delight them, whatever brings joy and satisfaction. To each being, he distributes treasures filling the entire continent of Jambudvīpa: gold, silver, lapis lazuli, crystal, agate, coral, amber, and all precious jewels. Moreover, he provides elephants, horses, carriages, and palaces constructed of seven jewels—towered mansions and pavilions. This great benefactor practices such charity for eighty years, until observing the aged condition of these beings—their white hair, wrinkled faces, sparse teeth, withered forms—he recognizes that death approaches. At that moment he thinks: 'These beings have aged considerably. I must now offer them the dharma and guide them toward enlightenment.'

"Gathering these beings, he proclaims to them the dharma of nirvāṇa, the essential truth. He teaches that all realms are impermanent, equal to foam and bubbles and burning flames, and that all should swiftly develop the determination to transcend cyclic existence. Upon hearing this dharma, all these beings directly attain arhatship, possessing the six divine penetrations, the three knowledges, and the eight deliverances, becoming entirely freed from the outflows of existence[^2].

"Maitreya, what is your assessment? Does this great benefactor's merit appear considerable?" Maitreya replied to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, this benefactor's merit is extraordinarily abundant! Even to distribute merely the objects of enjoyment to such countless beings would generate immeasurable merit. How much more so that he guides them to arhatship and complete liberation?"

The Buddha declared: "I shall explain explicitly. This benefactor distributes all objects of enjoyment to four hundred million billion nayuta world-realms' worth of six-path beings and leads them to arhatship. Yet if we compare this merit with that of a fiftieth person who hears the Lotus Sutra through the chain of five retransmissions and rejoices at even a single verse, the latter's merit exceeds the former by a hundred-fold, a thousand-fold, a million-fold, or immeasurable multiples thereof. Indeed, enumeration and analogy cannot capture the disparity. Maitreya, consider: the merit of a fiftieth person receiving the sutra through sequential rejoicing is already immeasurable and infinite. How much greater is the merit of one who hears it first-hand in the assembly and rejoices[^3]?"

Furthermore, Maitreya: "Should anyone come to a monastic dwelling with intention to hear the Lotus Sutra, and sit or stand to listen momentarily, the karmic fruits of such action shall cause that being to be reborn among gods and humans where they obtain excellent elephants, fine horses, precious conveyances, and celestial palaces[^4].

"Moreover, should one be in a place where the dharma is taught and another person arrives, and one encourages that second person to sit and listen to the dharma—or even offers one's own seat to them—the merit accrued causes that person to be reborn where they obtain the seat of Śakra Devendra, the seat of Brahma, or the seat of a Cakravartin king.

"Again, Maitreya: should someone say to another, 'There exists a sutra called the Lotus—let us go together to hear it taught.' And that person, receiving this encouragement, attends the teaching and hears it even briefly—the karmic fruition of such action is remarkable indeed. From that point onward, across successive rebirths, that person shall be reborn together with bodhisattvas who have attained dhāraṇī-powers. They shall possess keen intelligence and wisdom, never experiencing muteness for a hundred thousand lifetimes. Their breath shall never emit foul odor; their tongue shall not become diseased or disabled; their mouth shall be free from all illness. Their teeth shall neither become sparse nor yellow nor black, neither crooked nor gapped nor uneven nor protruding nor stunted nor blackened, neither unpleasant to behold nor defective. Their lips shall be neither thick nor thin, neither sunken nor protruding, neither cracked nor diseased nor scarred, neither deformed nor twisted nor thick nor large nor dark nor displeasing in any way. Their nose shall be straight, refined, and perfectly proportioned. Their face shall remain round and complete throughout their life. Their eyebrows shall be elevated and extended. Their forehead shall be broad, level, and perfectly symmetric. All marks of human perfection shall be complete. In each world where they are born, they shall perceive the Buddha, hear the dharma, and develop authentic faith in the teachings. This is the benefit that accrues when one person encourages another to hear the Lotus Sutra[^5]."

The Buddha continued: "Maitreya, consider the merit accruing from merely encouraging one person to attend the dharma-teaching. How much greater must be the merit of those who listen with unified attention, comprehend the dharma's meaning, and practice accordingly?"
## The Cascading Benefits of Rejoicing-Transmission
*At that time, the Tathāgata wished to restate this meaning through verse:*

A person hearing this dharma in the assembly,
Even a single verse, with sincere rejoicing,
Teaches it to another, who also rejoices,
The transmission continuing through fifty successive recipients—
The fifty-fold retransmission holds merit of this nature:

A great benefactor supplies all pleasures
To countless beings numbering like dust-grains,
Sustaining their delight through eighty full years,
Each receiving whatever their hearts desire.
Observing their aging—white hair, wrinkled faces,
Teeth sparse, forms withered and declining—
He thinks: "Death approaches these beings now.
I shall guide them through the dharma to liberation,"
And immediately teaches them the true dharma:
"All existence is impermanent as mist and foam and flame—
Each of you should cultivate the mind of renunciation."

All who heard this dharma-teaching
Attained arhatship directly,
Possessed six divine penetrations,
Three knowledges and eight deliverances[^6].

The last person in the fiftieth transmission
Who heard one verse with sincere joy—
That person's merit surpasses this benefactor's
Beyond all possible comparison or reckoning.

When retransmitted through successive hearers,
The accumulated merit remains immeasurable.
How much greater is the merit
Of those who heard first in the assembly?

If one person encourages another:
"This sutra called the Lotus Dharma is profound and subtle,
Encountered only once in thousands of eons—
Come, let us hear its teaching together!"

And that person, heeding the encouragement,
Attends to hear, even for a brief moment—
The fruit of such action shall now be explained:

Throughout the person's world-to-world rebirths,
The mouth shall bear no fault:
Teeth shall not be sparse, yellow, or black,
Lips shall not be thick, sunken, or broken,
The tongue shall not be parched, dark, or shortened,
The nose shall be refined, elevated, and perfectly straight,
The forehead broad and level and symmetric,
The face complete in all features,
Beloved by all who see them,
With breath naturally sweet-scented,
And fragrance of utpala blossoms
Shall emanate from the mouth itself[^7].

Those who intentionally visit a monastery
Desiring to hear the Lotus Sutra,
Finding joy in the teaching, even momentarily—
The karma-fruit is such as this:

Subsequently reborn in divine and human realms,
They obtain excellent elephants and horses,
Precious vehicles and palanquins,
And heavenly mansions as dwellings[^8].

Should one encourage another to sit and listen
To the dharma taught in that assembly,
Or share one's own seat with the listener—
The merit-result is to obtain in rebirth
The seat of Śakra the Lord,
The seat of Brahma,
Or the seat of a Cakravartin King[^9].

What need be said of one who hears with perfect attention,
Who understands the dharma's meaning thoroughly,
And practices exactly as the sutra instructs?
Such merit possesses no measure,
Boundless and infinite in its extent[^10].

## Philosophical Implications

Chapter 18 operates as a mirror and extension of Chapter 17, exploring the consequences of dharma-transmission from a different angle. Where Chapter 17 emphasized the astonishing merit accruing from a single moment of authentic recognition (hearing one verse and rejoicing), Chapter 18 emphasizes the exponential multiplication of merit through sequential transmission.

The chapter's central logical move involves what might be called "recursive equivalence." The merit of the fiftieth person in a transmission chain equals or exceeds the merit of a great benefactor who practices unimaginable charity. Yet this second benefactor surpasses the first in effort and intention. Why this reversal of proportionality?

The text suggests that dharma-transmission operates according to principles foreign to conventional causality. When material goods are distributed, the act completes; the giver has less, the receiver has more. But when dharma is transmitted, something peculiar occurs: the transmitter retains the dharma while the receiver also gains it. Moreover, the receiver's joy (rejoicing) is not diminished by sharing but magnified through the act of communication. The fifty-fold retransmission does not dissipate the original teaching but concentrates it through repeated recognition.

The architectural components repeated throughout this chapter—the enormous benefactor providing seventy-two varieties of precious substances to universe-spanning populations—function as a counter-example. The text deliberately inflates this hypothetical to the point of absurdity. Four hundred million billion nayuta worlds, each populated with sentient beings numbering like dust-grains, each supported for eighty years with treasures from the seven jewels: even this immeasurable generosity pales before the simple fact of hearing one verse and rejoicing.

This apparent contradiction reveals a fundamental Buddhist insight regarding the nature of merit and spiritual value. Conventional merit (dana-maya, virtue through giving) addresses suffering in cyclic existence; it improves circumstances within the world. Ultimate merit (dharma-jnana, wisdom through dharma-recognition) addresses the structure of cyclic existence itself. A being who gives treasure for eighty years to universe-spanning populations still operates within the framework of cyclical existence, still accumulates conventional karma. A being who hears one verse and recognizes its truth begins to transcend that framework entirely.

The text also introduces a crucial Buddhist principle regarding transmission-chains: that later hearers need not be "less enlightened" than earlier ones. In many religious systems, the founder carries unique authority; later teachers are mere conduits. The Lotus Sutra suggests something radically different—that each act of transmission is a fresh encounter with dharma-truth, equally vivifying, equally transformative. The fiftieth person is not a secondary recipient but a primary one. Their rejoicing is not a pale echo of the first person's response but an equally authentic response to the same eternal truth.

The physical benefits enumerated—dental perfection, vocal sweetness, freedom from stuttering—again appear to represent symbolic transformations signifying readiness for dharma-transmission. These are not cosmetic improvements but functional enhancements. A dharma-teacher must speak clearly, be heard without difficulty, be remembered by listeners. These mundane physical attributes become instruments of sacred communication.

The text also pioneers a sophisticated Buddhist understanding of "encouragement" (upapreksha) as dharmic action. To encourage another to hear the dharma is presented as carrying merit equivalent to direct participation in that hearing. This suggests that facilitating dharma-transmission carries its own spiritual efficacy. The person who merely says "let us go hear the sutra together" participates in dharma-transmission as surely as one who sits in the assembly. This opens pathways of merit to those who lack the capacity for direct practice—the disabled, the elderly, the socially isolated—who can still participate through encouragement.

# Chapter 19: The Dharma-Teacher's Merits

## The Six Perfected Senses of the Dharma-Teacher
At that time, the Buddha addressed Avalokiteśvara the Bodhisattva of Constant Exertion: "When a virtuous man or woman maintains this Lotus Sutra—whether by reading it, reciting it, explaining it, or writing it—that person shall acquire eight hundred eye-perfections, one thousand two hundred ear-perfections, eight hundred nose-perfections, one thousand two hundred tongue-perfections, eight hundred body-perfections, and one thousand two hundred mind-perfections[^1]. Through these perfections, the six sense-organs shall become completely purified.

"The virtuous man or woman, having such purification, shall perceive through the naturally-born physical eye—unenhanced by supernatural power—the three thousand great thousand-worlds in all their extent and interior. They perceive mountains and forests, great rivers and seas, extending downward to Avīci hell and upward to the Akaniṣṭha realm. All beings inhabiting these realms, together with their karmic deeds, causes, and destinies, the virtuous person sees and knows completely[^2].

"Furthermore, Avalokiteśvara, when a virtuous man or woman maintains this sutra—whether reading, reciting, explaining, or writing it—they acquire one thousand two hundred ear-perfections. The naturally-born ear, without supernatural augmentation, shall hear the three thousand great thousand-worlds in all their dimensions. They shall perceive:

"All animal sounds—the trumpeting of elephants, neighing of horses, bellowing of cattle, bleating of sheep, and cries of other beasts. The sounds of carriages and carts. The cries of human suffering and lamentation, the sounds of sorrow and anguish. The sounds of spiral shells and drums, bells and chimes, flutes and pipes, stringed instruments and musical instruments of every description. The sounds of song and speech, the voices of men and women, of young boys and young girls. Dharmic sounds and non-dharmic sounds. Sounds of suffering and sounds of joy. The sounds of common beings and the voices of sages. Sounds expressing pleasure and sounds expressing displeasure.

"They shall hear the voices of gods and the songs of celestial beings, the voices of dragons and serpent-beings, the cries of night-roaming spirits, the voices of gandharvas and asuras, of kinnara-musicians and great serpents, the sounds of fire and water and wind, the groans from hell, the voices of hungry ghosts, and the sounds of animal realms. The voices of bhikṣus and bhikṣuṇīs reciting sutras, the voices of those who have heard the dharma completely, the voices of solitary buddhas.

"They shall hear the voices of bodhisattvas reading and reciting the dharma, explaining it to others, and analyzing its meaning. They shall hear the voices of all buddhas in all the ten directions, expounding the dharma in their assemblies. The dharma-maintainer, dwelling in this Sahā world, hears all of these voices without limit, and their ear-faculty is not harmed or confused in this perception. Though not possessing the supernatural ear-penetration, merely through the naturally-born ear purified by maintaining this sutra, the dharma-teacher hears all these sounds simultaneously, perfectly discriminating their natures[^3].

"Moreover, Avalokiteśvara, when a virtuous man or woman maintains this sutra—whether reading, reciting, explaining, or writing it—they acquire eight hundred nose-perfections. The naturally-born nostril, though not possessing supernatural smell-penetration, shall perceive from above and below, from near and far, all scents within the three thousand great thousand-worlds[^4].

"Such scents include: the fragrances of sumana flowers, jasmine flowers, and other blossoms; the scents of tree-blossoms and fruit-trees; the fragrances of sandalwood, aloeswood, tamālapattra, and tagara; the scents of thousands upon thousands of incenses and perfumes, whether in powdered form, pill form, or anointing oil—all these scents the person perceives and discriminates perfectly.

"The dharma-maintainer perceives the scents of human bodies—those of men and women, youth and maidens—and from near or far, whether approaching or departing, identifies the beings by their scent. The dharma-maintainer perceives the scents of turumeric-trees and other fragrant trees, perceives all scents throughout the cosmos from the greatest depths to the highest realms. They perceive the scents of heavenly incenses and perfumes, the scents of heavenly flowers and celestial sandalwood, and of all beings in all realms—whether gods, nagas, or other celestial beings. The dharma-maintainer perceives all these scents without confusion and can precisely explain them to others without error[^5].

"Furthermore, Avalokiteśvara, when a virtuous man or woman maintains this sutra—whether reading, reciting, explaining, or writing it—they acquire one thousand two hundred tongue-perfections. Whatever flavors enter the mouth—whether pleasing or displeasing, delicious or bitter, pungent or astringent—all are transformed into the supreme taste, becoming sweet as the amrita of the divine realms. No unpleasant flavor exists[^6].

"When such a person speaks the dharma before a great assembly, their speech produces a profound and subtle voice that naturally penetrates the hearts of all listeners, bringing them joy and delight. All celestial beings—gods and goddesses, Śakra and Brahma, the divine assembly—hearing this profound and subtle voice, assemble to listen with reverence. Dragons and nagas, night-spirits and gandharvas, asuras, kinnara-musicians, and great serpents, all come near, showing respect and honor. Monks and nuns, male and female lay followers, kings and princes, ministers and attendants, small and great Cakravartin kings with their thousand sons and retinues, all bring celestial palaces and come to hear the teaching. Through the dharma-teacher's skillful exposition, Brahmins, householders, and all people of the realm give service and support throughout their lives.

"All śrāvakas and solitary buddhas, bodhisattvas, and buddhas constantly delight in seeing such a person. All the buddhas in the regions where such a person dwells direct their teaching toward that person. All these buddhas entrust their dharma to that person, and all dharma established by all buddhas becomes available to them. Such a person's consciousness becomes perfectly clear, and they can speak profound and subtle dharma-voices, reaching any distance. Whatever they speak becomes consistent with what all buddhas have taught in the past; nothing they say is false or inconsistent[^7].

"Finally, Avalokiteśvara, when a virtuous man or woman maintains this sutra—whether reading, reciting, explaining, or writing it (particularly after the Buddha's passing)—they acquire one thousand two hundred mind-perfections. The mind-faculty becomes so refined that upon hearing even a single verse or a single phrase, the person comprehends immeasurable and boundless significance. Once comprehending the meaning, they can then explain one verse or one phrase through a single month, four months, or even an entire year, expounding its meaning according to principle. All their discourse is consistent with the dharma and conforms to true reality[^8].

"If they speak of worldly affairs, political administration, or practical matters of livelihood, all such speech accords with the dharma and is entirely true and genuine. The mind becomes so refined that they perceive the thoughts, intents, and mental preoccupations of all six-path beings inhabiting the three thousand great thousand-worlds. Though not yet possessing unreleased wisdom, such a person's mind-faculty is purified to this degree.

"Whatever the person thinks, ponders, and speaks becomes dharmic teaching—entirely true and never false. These teachings are identical to those taught by all the buddhas of the past. Because such a person speaks the dharma, they possess no fear before any assembly. All beings recognize them as one who has approached the way-place; they will not long delay before attaining unsurpassed, perfect enlightenment and bringing benefit to countless beings[^9]."
## The Enumeration of Dharma-Teacher's Powers
*At that time, the Tathāgata wished to restate this meaning through verse:*

When a person speaks this sutra
Before a great assembly without fear,
Listen to the perfections they obtain:

Eight hundred eye-perfections they achieve,
Granting the eyes extraordinary clarity.
Through the naturally-born eye alone,
They perceive all of the three thousand worlds,
The interior and exterior mountains—Sumeru and Cakravada—
The iron-circling ranges, and all terrestrial features,
The vast oceans and flowing rivers,
Down to the Great Hell,
Up to the Akaniṣṭha realm,
Seeing all beings in between,
And perfectly knowing their karma and destinies.
Though not yet possessing the divine eye,
The power of the naturally-born eye is such as this[^10].

One thousand two hundred ear-perfections they obtain,
Achieving extraordinary auditory clarity.
Through the naturally-born ear alone,
They hear all sounds within the three thousand worlds:
The sounds of elephants, horses, cattle, and sheep,
The cries of carts and chariots,
The sounds of weeping and lamentation,
The sounds of sorrow and anguish,
The calls of conches and beating of drums,
The sounds of bells and tinkling chimes,
The music of harps, zithers, and stringed instruments,
The sounds of flutes and pipes and wind-instruments,
The clear sounds of excellent singing,
Hearing these without attachment,
The countless sounds of humans speaking,
Understanding all voices completely[^11].

They also hear divine voices singing,
The subtle songs of celestial melodies,
And the voices of male and female beings,
Of young boys and young maidens.
From the mountain valleys and deep ravines,
The sounds of kalaviṅka-birds and other species,
The magnificent cries of various birds,
The sounds of those in the Hell realms,
The suffering screams of tortured beings,
And the cries of hungry ghosts,
Driven by hunger and thirst to seek food,
The voices of the asura-realm,
Dwelling at the edges of the great oceans,
Speaking among themselves in loud voices[^12].

Such a dharma-teacher, dwelling in this place,
Hears all these sounds from afar,
Yet the ear-faculty remains unharmed.
From the ten directions and all world-systems,
Cries of beasts answering one another,
The dharma-teacher hears them all[^13].

In the Brahma realms and higher,
The light-sphere and immeasurable-light realms,
Up to the highest realm of existence,
The divine speech of beings dwelling there,
The dharma-teacher, standing here,
Hears perfectly all these voices.
All monks and nuns,
And all those reading scripture,
Or explaining it to others—
The dharma-teacher hears them all.
Likewise, all bodhisattvas,
Reading and reciting the dharma,
Or explaining it to others,
Or analyzing its meanings—
All such voices the teacher hears perfectly[^14].

All the buddhas of all directions,
Possessed of the hundred marks of beauty,
Teaching all sentient beings,
The dharma-teacher hears their voices completely,
While remaining in this place.
From the superb and subtle speech,
The dharma-teacher is able to hear
All these sounds without injury to the ear-organ,
And the keenness of the ear is such
That all voices can be understood and known.

The dharma-teacher thus equipped
Though not yet possessing the divine ear,
Merely from this naturally-born ear,
Such are the merits already present[^15].

The person's nose becomes quite pure,
And through this world in particular,
All scents, whether fragrant or foul,
The person perceives and knows completely.
From sumana and from jasmine flowers,
And from other fragrant blossoms,
From fragrant woods and fruited trees,
Sandalwood, aloeswood-wood, and other aromatics,
From the various sandalwood species,
And the countless mixed incenses—
Both powdered form and pill-shaped form,
And anointing oils—
The dharma-teacher, dwelling here, can distinguish them all[^16].

Also the scents of all beings—
Elephant scents, horse scents, cattle scents,
The scents of men and scents of women,
Youth and maiden fragrances,
And understanding all these scents from afar,
Whether drawing near or moving away,
The dharma-teacher knows the location through scent.
From mighty monarchs and lesser kings,
And the sons and ministers thereof,
All the precious adornments,
And underground treasure-stores,
The royal harem with its fragrances—
All these the teacher knows by scent[^17].

The dharma-teacher smells beautiful flowers,
Both heavenly and earthly blossoms,
And the sandal-trees and great forests—
All plants and flowers blooming there,
The dharma-teacher, dwelling in this realm,
Understands all their locations.
From Brahma realms and higher spheres,
Even to the highest existence realm,
The heavenly incenses and perfumes,
The heavenly fragrances of celestial flowers—
All such heavenly scents are perceived and known.
And understanding the scents of all beings—
Gods, dragons, and celestial folk—
Their locations are known.
Though not yet possessing bodhisattva's
Unreleased-wisdom-generated nose,
Already such is the power obtained[^18].

The person's tongue becomes quite pure,
Never receiving any unpleasant taste:
Whatever enters the mouth—
The pleasant and the unpleasant,
Whatever is eaten—
All becomes supreme taste,
Like the divine amrita nectar itself,
Where nothing is unpleasant.
When expounding the dharma,
From the mouth emerges profound voice,
Able to enter all hearts,
Bringing all who hear to joy and happiness[^19].

Heavenly devas and their consorts,
Śakra and Lord Brahma,
And all the assembled deities,
Come to hear the dharma being taught.
Dragons and nagas alike,
Night-wandering spirits and gandharvas,
Asuras, kinnaras, and great serpents—
They all approach with respect and honor[^20].

All bhikṣu and bhikṣuṇī crowds,
And lay followers, male and female,
Kings and royal princes,
Ministers and palace attendants,
Small and great Cakravartin Kings,
With a thousand sons and full retinues,
Bringing forth celestial palaces,
All come together to hear the teaching.
Through the dharma-teacher's skill in explaining,
Brahmins, householders, and all common folk
Give their service throughout their lives.
All śrāvakas and solitary buddhas,
Bodhisattvas and the buddhas themselves,
Constantly take joy in seeing such a person[^21].

In all the world-regions where they dwell,
The buddhas all turn their attention there.
Toward such a dharma-teacher's presence,
All the buddhas direct their teaching.
All dharma that all buddhas possess
Becomes available to such a person.
Whatever they speak always proves,
To be Buddha-dharma, never false.
Because they speak such dharma,
They face all assemblies without any fear[^22].

For this dharma-teacher's good fortune,
When first encountering this dharma,
One should scatter heavenly flowers,
Should cover the body with heavenly garments,
Should bow the head to their feet,
And cultivate a mind-attitude of Buddha-reverence.

Furthermore, consider this well:
This person shall not long remain
Before reaching the enlightenment-place,
Obtaining unsurpassed wisdom,
And broadly benefiting all beings
Of the human and celestial realms.
The location where they dwell and teach—
Standing, sitting, walking, or resting,
Even when speaking a single verse—
That place should have a stupa erected,
Adorned with elaborate beauty,
And honored with manifold offerings.
In such places dwell enlightenment-beings,
Constantly dwelling there in coming and going,
In sitting and sleeping positions.

## Philosophical Implications

Chapter 19 represents a theological milestone in the Lotus Sutra's argument: the elevation of dharma-teachers to Buddha-equivalence. Where earlier chapters established that all beings possess Buddha-nature and can attain enlightenment, Chapter 19 specifies what happens to those who devote themselves to maintaining and teaching this supreme sutra.

The chapter constructs a meticulous taxonomy of sensory perfection, detailing how each of the six senses—sight, hearing, smell, taste, touch (implicit in the body-perfection), and mind—becomes transformed through dharma-maintenance. This anatomical approach to enlightenment contradicts the notion that liberation is merely mental or spiritual. The text insists that dharma-transformation permeates the entire organism.

The eye-perfections manifest as unobstructed vision of all three-thousand-worlds simultaneously. In Buddhist cosmology, the three thousand great thousand-worlds (trisahara-mahasahasra-lokadhatu) represents all sentient existence. The dharma-maintainer perceives not merely physical geography but the karmic situations of all beings—their deeds, causes, and destinies. This is significant because it suggests that dharma-penetration includes moral-causal understanding. One who truly comprehends the dharma perceives the entire structure of cause-and-effect that binds existence.

The ear-perfections catalogs an extraordinary range of sounds: animal cries, human voices in all emotional registers, divine music, the groans of hell-beings. Importantly, the text specifies that this comprehensive auditory perception does not overwhelm or confuse the teacher. They hear all sounds simultaneously yet maintain perfect discrimination. This suggests that dharma-consciousness transcends the normal human limitation of sequential auditory processing.

The nose-perfections describe the capacity to perceive scents across all realms, identifying beings and substances by fragrance. The olfactory metaphor deserves careful attention. In Buddhist symbolism, fragrance often represents the spreading of dharma. That the teacher perceives all scents reflects their capacity to recognize dharma-presence throughout all realms, even in places where dharma is not explicitly taught.

The tongue-perfections involve two transformations: first, the alchemical conversion of all tastes into divine nectar (amrita), suggesting that dharma-consciousness renders all experience sacred; second, the capacity for profound speech that penetrates hearts and brings joy. This combination of inner transformation and outer expression indicates that the dharma-teacher's speech is not merely informative but spiritually transformative.

The mind-perfections represent the sutra's deepest claim about the dharma-teacher's consciousness. A single verse yields infinite meanings (pratibhana, inexhaustible eloquence). The teacher can expound that single verse for a month, four months, or a year, finding new layers of significance without exhaustion. This reflects the Buddhist principle that the dharma possesses infinite depth—that sacred text is not merely literary but ontologically inexhaustible.

Most strikingly, the text declares that the dharma-teacher's speech, even when discussing mundane affairs, accords with dharma and truth. This is because the teacher's consciousness has become so thoroughly saturated with dharmic understanding that even ordinary communication reflects ultimate reality. There is no longer a division between dharma-speech and ordinary speech; all speech becomes dharmic.

The chapter also introduces a crucial principle: that dharma-teachers become recipients of Buddha-authorization. "All the buddhas entrust their dharma to such a person." This suggests that across all time and space, all Buddhas recognize and support those who maintain the Lotus Sutra. The dharma-teacher is not merely an individual practitioner but a node in a vast network of Buddha-activity.

*[End of Fascicle 6 (卷六)]*

# Chapter 20: The Bodhisattva of Never-Despiser

## The Eternal Practitioner of Universal Respect
At that time, the Buddha addressed the Bodhisattva Mahāsthāmaprāpta: "You should understand that among those who maintain the Lotus Sutra, should any individual curse, revile, or disparage such a keeper of the sutra, the karmic consequences shall be severe, as described previously. But understand equally: those who maintain this Lotus Sutra thereby acquire the perfected senses mentioned—eye-purity, ear-purity, nose-purity, tongue-purity, body-purity, and mind-purity[^1].

"Mahāsthāmaprāpta, in ages long past—across immeasurable, incalculable eons—there existed a Buddha named Majestic Voice King, the Tathāgata, the Arhat, the Perfectly Awakened One, the One Perfectly Accomplished in Knowledge and Conduct, the Sugata, the Knower of the World, the Unsurpassable Guide, the Tamer of the Untamed, the Teacher of Gods and Humans, the Buddha, the World-Honored One. His lifespan extended across four million billion nayuta eons. In his world, an age called the Age of Prosperity prevailed, and his realm was named the Great Achievement.

"The Buddha Majestic Voice King taught the dharma to gods, humans, and asuras. To those seeking arhatship, he explained the dharma of the four truths, enabling them to transcend birth, old age, sickness, and death, ultimately reaching the final peace of nirvāṇa. To those seeking solitary-buddha realization, he taught the dharma of dependent origination in twelve aspects. To the bodhisattvas and all beings, he explained the dharma of the six perfections, ultimately leading them to perfect enlightenment[^2].

"In that age, the Buddha Majestic Voice King possessed eighty billion great bodhisattvas and assemblies of seventy-two trillion enlightened śrāvakas. That Buddha's lifespan extended four million billion nayuta eons. The dharma remained as the Buddha's living teaching for a period of years equal in number to the dust-particles of an entire Jambudvīpa continent. The semblance dharma (the era after the Buddha's departure when the form of the teachings remains but their living force diminishes) also persisted through numbers of years equal to the dust-particles of an entire continent. After the correct dharma and semblance dharma had been exhausted, in that realm there appeared destructive, arrogant monks. During this degenerate epoch, the true dharma had become obscured.

"At that time, within this degraded assembly, there was a bodhisattva-monk named Never-Despiser. Why was he called Never-Despiser? Because whenever he encountered monks, nuns, laymen, or laywomen of any station, he would immediately bow in reverence, praise them, and speak these words: 'I profoundly honor you. I dare not belittle or despise you. Why? Because you all practice the bodhisattva path. You shall inevitably attain Buddhahood.' This Never-Despiser Bodhisattva did not devote himself primarily to reading and reciting the sutras but concentrated entirely on the practice of reverence. Whenever he encountered the four assemblies from a distance, he would deliberately approach them, prostrate himself before them, and praise them with the same declaration: 'I dare not despise you. You are all destined to become Buddhas.' Those among these four assemblies who possessed conceit or impure minds would revile him, saying: 'This ignorant monk! Why does he presume to declare that we shall become Buddhas? What authority does he have to grant us such predictions? We do not want such false prophecies!' So Never-Despiser endured decades of abuse and vilification without becoming angry. His practice remained constant: 'I dare not despise you. You are destined for Buddhahood.'

"When these contentious monks, nuns, and laypeople approached with sticks, stones, and tiles, attempting to strike him, Never-Despiser would flee to a distance, continuing to proclaim loudly: 'I dare not despise you. You shall become Buddhas!' Because of his constant proclamation of this message, both the arrogant monks and the four assemblies came to call him 'Never-Despiser'[^3].

"When the end of this bodhisattva's life approached, he heard suspended in the void the voice of the Buddha Majestic Voice King reciting the Lotus Sutra—specifically the dharma of all twenty thousand million billion verses from the first exposition. Never-Despiser absorbed and retained all these verses completely. He immediately acquired the six perfected senses: eye-clarity, ear-clarity, nose-clarity, tongue-clarity, body-clarity, and mind-clarity, exactly as previously described[^4]. Having attained these purified senses, his lifespan was extended two hundred billion nayuta years, and he proceeded to widely expound the Lotus Sutra for the benefit of all beings.

"At that time, those arrogant monks, nuns, and laypeople who had previously reviled him—observing that he possessed great supernatural powers, eloquent wisdom-speech, and profound tranquility—abandoned their contempt and began to follow him. This Never-Despiser Bodhisattva thus transformed millions of billions of beings, establishing them in unsurpassed perfect enlightenment.

"When Never-Despiser ultimately completed his remaining lifespan, he encountered two billion buddhas, all called the Sunny-Moon Radiant Buddhas, in whose dharma he received, maintained, read, and recited this Lotus Sutra. Because he always proclaimed this sutra among the four assemblies, he attained the six purified senses. Later, he encountered another two billion buddhas, all called the Cloud-Radiance Kings of Liberation Buddhas, in whose dharma he again received, maintained, read, and recited this sutra. Through his proclamation of the dharma to the four assemblies, he continued developing the six purified senses.

"Subsequently, Never-Despiser encountered countless billions of buddhas, through whom he continued to receive, maintain, read, recite, and proclaim this Lotus Sutra, until his merits were completed and he attained perfect enlightenment.

"Mahāsthāmaprāpta, do you comprehend the meaning of this account? The Never-Despiser Bodhisattva of that ancient time—he was none other than my own previous embodiment! Had I not received, maintained, read, recited, and proclaimed this Lotus Sutra to others in my past existences, I could never have attained swift enlightenment. The four assemblies of that era—the monks, nuns, male and female laypeople—those who despised me: through contempt, they passed two hundred billion eons never encountering a Buddha, never hearing the dharma, never seeing the sangha. Only after exhausting this karmic consequence did they begin again meeting the Never-Despiser Bodhisattva, who guided them toward unsurpassed enlightenment.

"Mahāsthāmaprāpta, consider this: the Lotus Sutra provides immeasurable benefit to all bodhisattvas and great beings. It swiftly carries them to unsurpassed perfect enlightenment. For this reason, all bodhisattvas, after the Buddha's passing, should constantly receive, maintain, read, recite, explain, and write this sutra[^5]."
## The Story of the Perpetual Reverent One
*At that time, the Tathāgata wished to restate this meaning through verse:*

In ages past, there was a Buddha
Called Majestic Voice King,
Possessed of supernatural wisdom,
Who guided countless beings.
Gods and humans and asuras alike
Received his universal honor and worship.

This Buddha having reached parinirvāṇa,
When the true dharma was nearly exhausted,
The arrogant and contentious monks
Possessed tremendous power.

At that time appeared a bodhisattva,
Named the Never-Despiser,
Who came before all beings—
Monks, nuns, male and female laypeople—
And spoke these words of respect:

"I dare not despise you all,
Because you practice the bodhisattva path
And shall inevitably achieve Buddhahood."

The contentious monks and assembly
Replied with anger and disdain:
"Who granted you such wisdom
To declare our future enlightenment?
We need not your false declarations!"

Yet Never-Despiser, enduring this abuse
Through many years of practice,
Continued speaking his message of respect:
"You shall become Buddhas!"

When struck with sticks and stones,
The bodhisattva would withdraw,
Yet continue from a distance proclaiming:
"I do not despise you—
You shall become Buddhas!"

Through such constant proclamation,
The monks, nuns, and lay assemblies
Named him "the Never-Despiser."

As his final moments approached,
He heard in the void
The Buddha Medicine King's voice
Reciting the Lotus Sutra's verses—
Twenty million billion verses in total[^6].

Having received these complete verses,
His six sense-organs became purified,
His eye-sight became perfectly clear,
His hearing became keenly acute,
His sense of smell became perfectly refined,
His taste-perception became supremely exquisite,
His body-sensitivity became completely refined,
His mind became penetratingly clear[^7].

His lifespan was extended
Two hundred billion nayuta years,
And he proceeded to widely expound
This Lotus Sutra throughout the world.

The arrogant four assemblies,
Witnessing his great supernatural powers,
His eloquent dharma-speech,
His profound peaceful mastery,
All abandoned their contempt
And followed him as devoted disciples.

The Never-Despiser Bodhisattva
Thus led millions of billions of beings
To unsurpassed perfect enlightenment.

After his passing,
He encountered two billion buddhas,
All bearing the name Sunny-Moon Radiance,
In whose teachings he received and maintained
This Lotus Sutra,
Proclaiming it to the four assemblies,
Thus continuously perfecting his six senses.

He then encountered another two billion buddhas,
All called Cloud-Radiance Liberation Kings,
In whose dharma he again received, maintained,
And widely proclaimed this sutra
To countless assemblies,
Further developing the six purified senses.

He subsequently encountered countless billions of buddhas,
Through whom he continuously received and maintained
This Lotus Sutra,
Proclaiming it through innumerable assemblies,
Until his merits were fully perfected
And he attained perfect enlightenment[^8].

The Never-Despiser of ancient times—
He is none other than I myself!
Had I not received and maintained
This Lotus Sutra
In my former lives,
Had I not proclaimed it to others,
I could never have swiftly attained
This supreme enlightenment.

Those four assemblies of that age,
The monks, nuns, laymen, and laywomen—
Those who despised the Never-Despiser:
Through their contempt,
They were unable for two hundred billion eons
To encounter any Buddha,
To hear any dharma,
To perceive any sangha community.

Only after that karmic consequence was exhausted
Did they encounter the Never-Despiser again,
Who gradually guided them
Toward unsurpassed enlightenment[^9].

## Philosophical Implications

Chapter 20 represents the practical embodiment of all preceding teachings. The Never-Despiser story answers a question implicit throughout the sutra: "What does dharma-realization look like in concrete practice?" The answer is stunning in its simplicity and universality.

The chapter's brilliance lies in its apparent paradox. Never-Despiser practices contrary to Buddhist convention: he does not study sutras intensively, does not practice meditation, does not seek supernatural powers. Instead, he engages in what appears to be the simplest practice imaginable—reverence toward all beings. Yet through this single practice, he transforms himself and countless others.

The principle underlying Never-Despiser's practice is Buddha-nature universalism taken to radical extremes. Rather than recognizing Buddha-nature as a theoretical doctrine ("all beings possess Buddha-nature and can attain enlightenment"), Never-Despiser acts as if all beings have already achieved enlightenment. His proclamation "You shall become Buddhas!" is not a prediction of future attainment but a present-tense recognition. He sees through the degraded surface to the eternal Buddha-reality beneath.

The response to Never-Despiser's practice reveals the profound difficulty of recognizing one's own Buddha-nature. Those who despise him—who revile, strike, and curse him—are actually harming themselves. The text explicitly states that through contempt, they create the karma that separates them from Buddha-encounter for immense eons. Yet even this separation is eventually overcome. The Never-Despiser's dharma-transmission ultimately reaches even those who despised him, gradually transforming them.

This creates a paradoxical temporality. Never-Despiser encounters beings who are simultaneously in two conditions: they are future Buddhas (which is what Never-Despiser sees), yet they are currently unable to recognize this truth. They must undergo two hundred billion eons of karmic separation from Buddha-truth before they can receive the bodhisattva's teaching. The future and the present overlay one another; enlightenment exists eternally while its actualization in consciousness unfolds temporally.

# Chapter 21: The Tathāgata's Supernatural Powers

## The Cosmic Demonstration of Dharma-Transmission
At that time, myriads of bodhisattvas—numbering like the dust-particles of entire world-realms—emerged from beneath the earth. These were bodhisattvas who had previously dwelt in the Sahā world. They approached the Buddha, bowed with their heads to his feet, and addressed him: "World-Honored One, after you enter final nirvāṇa, in the lands where your various emanations have taught and subsequently passed away, we shall extensively proclaim this Lotus Sutra. Why? Because we ourselves desire to obtain this true, pure, supreme dharma. We shall receive it, maintain it, read and recite it, explain it, and offer reverent worship to it[^1]."

At that moment, the Tathāgata revealed his great supernatural powers before all assembled—before myriads of old bodhisattvas dwelling in the Sahā world, before myriads of monks and nuns, before male and female lay followers, before gods, dragons, and all types of supernatural beings. The Buddha extended his broad, long tongue upward until it reached the Brahma heavens, and from every pore of his body, he emitted innumerable rays of light, illuminating all ten directions of the world-system. All the precious trees beneath which were seated the Buddhas from the ten directions, as well as Śākyamuni Buddha and the eternally present Buddha (Prabhūtaratna), similarly extended their broad, long tongues and emitted vast rays of light[^2]. The display of supernatural power continued for one hundred thousand years.

Afterward, the Buddha and all those Buddhas withdrew their tongues. At that moment, all the Buddhas produced a single sound of coughing and a simultaneous finger-snap. These two sounds echoed throughout the ten directions of all world-systems. In all those world-systems, the very earth underwent six kinds of trembling: trembling, quaking, roaring, cracking, heaving, and surging upward. Through the Buddha's supernatural power, all beings throughout the ten directions perceived this Sahā world-realm: they saw the endless precious trees beneath which the Buddhas sat, and they perceived Śākyamuni Buddha together with the eternally present Buddha, surrounded by infinite assemblies of bodhisattvas and the four categories of disciples—all offering reverent worship[^3].

All the celestial beings then proclaimed aloud in the void: "Beyond myriads of incalculable billion-billion world-systems, there exists a realm named Sahā. Within it dwells a Buddha called Śākyamuni, who now teaches all bodhisattvas a great Mahāyāna sutra named the Lotus Sutra, teaching the bodhisattva-path, guarded and protected by all the Buddhas. You should all generate profound reverence, offer sincere worship, and respectfully honor Śākyamuni Buddha!"

When those innumerable beings heard this proclamation, all brought their palms together toward the Sahā world. They offered varieties of flowers, fragrances, garlands, silk banners, jeweled canopies, and precious objects from the ten directions. All these offered substances rose through the air like clouds accumulating, transforming into precious canopies that covered the Buddhas of the ten directions[^4]. In this moment, the ten directions became unified and unobstructed, appearing as a single Buddha-realm.

At that time, the Buddha addressed the bodhisattva assembly led by Viśiṣṭa Bodhisattva: "The supernatural power of all the Buddhas is immeasurable, incalculable, boundless, and inconceivable. If I were to spend immeasurable, incalculable billions upon billions of eons explaining the supernatural powers, the merit, and the benefit of this sutra, I could never exhaust them. Therefore, in brief: all the dharmas possessed by the Tathāgata, all the sovereign powers of the Tathāgata, and all the profoundest secret treasures of the Tathāgata, all the exceedingly profound matters of the Tathāgata—all are revealed and proclaimed in this sutra. For this reason, after the Tathāgata's passing, you should receive, maintain, read, recite, explain, and write this sutra in all lands. In any land where this sutra is received and maintained—whether in a monastic garden, forest, tree-shade, monastic dwelling, or lay residence, whether in a hall or pavilion, in mountain valleys or wilderness—all such places should have stupas erected and offerings presented. Why? Because you should understand that all such places are bodhi-seats. It is in such places that all the Buddhas have attained unsurpassed, perfect enlightenment. It is in such places that all the Buddhas have turned the dharma-wheel. It is in such places that all the Buddhas have entered final nirvāṇa[^5]."
## The Cosmic Verification of Dharma
*At that time, the Tathāgata wished to restate this meaning through verse:*

All the Buddhas, saviors of the world,
Possess great supernatural powers,
Manifesting boundless miraculous deeds
To delight all sentient beings.

The tongue-faculty extends to the Brahma realms,
The body emits countless rays of light,
All to establish the bodhisattva path,
Revealing these extraordinary phenomena.

The sound of the Buddhas coughing,
And the sound of their finger-snapping,
Echo throughout the ten world-directions,
While the earth trembles with six kinds of motion.

Through the supernatural power of the Buddha,
All enable the maintenance of this sutra,
All the Buddhas rejoice together,
Revealing boundless supernatural powers[^6].

The merit and virtue of this person
Possesses no limits or boundary,
Like the endless space extending
In all ten directions without obstruction[^7].

Those who maintain this sutra
Have already seen me,
Have also perceived the Buddha Prabhūtaratna,
And all the emanation-Buddhas alike.

They have seen me in this present moment
Teaching the bodhisattva assembly.
Those who maintain this sutra
Enable me and my emanations,
Enable the eternally present Buddha,
All together to rejoice.

The Buddhas of the ten directions—
Both those of the present moment,
And those of the three times (past, present, future)—
All are seen and honored,
All receive offerings and attain joy[^8].

The Buddhas seated at the bodhi-tree,
The supreme secret dharma they obtained—
Those who maintain this sutra
Shall not long remain before obtaining them[^9].

Those who maintain this sutra
